Serial Spine Vs Lateral Dislocate

\n
\n
\n
\n\nCallan_S \n\n\n
\n
\n\nMarch 2013 \n edited March 2013 in Game Design Help
\n
\n
\n
\n
\n This might be useful for designers to read, so I'm popping it here.

First off, we'll start with an example which is a radical departure from traditional RPG's. Basically it's a very simple, but complete solo RPG. The following are the steps of play.\n
1. As a player you have 10 HP in the game - these represent a fictional character.
2. You have a travel distance of 20 kilometers you need to make to complete this game. Roll 1D6 - this is how far you traveled in kilometeres. Record the amount. But if you roll a 1, also a monster encounter occurs - go to step #3. If no encounter occured, go to step #4.

3. Roll 1D6 for your character and 1D6 for the monster. If your roll is equal to or higher than the monsters roll, you beat it and can continue to step #4. If the monsters roll is higher, you lose 1 HP. If you lose all 10 HP, you lose the game and the game ends. Keep rolling until either you or the monster is beaten.

3A: Also there is another player present called a GM. You may describe a fictional account of your attack on the monster and if he feels moved to do so, he may grant you +2 added upon your dice roll.
3B: If you still roll lower than the monster, you keep the +2 until you pass a roll against the monster.

#4. If you make it to 20 kilometers, you have won the game! Congrats! Otherwise go back to step #2.
\n
The above is an example of a Serial Spine. You can see it takes you all the way right from the start of play, through to the end.

Now we'll contrast that against roughly the same elements, in Lateral Dislocate form\n
Chapter 1: Character Generation
Your character has 10 HP.

Chapter 2: Combat
Characters roll 1D6 vs each other, the highest roll wins and the loser loses 1 HP.

Also the GM may grant a character +2 to hit, if it seems warranted.

Chapter 3: Monsters
Monsters have 1 HP each

Chapter 4: Adventures
Adventures are about traveling 20 kilometers. Generally traveling 1D6 kilometers at a time.

Chapter 5: GM advice
About 16% of the time monsters show up
\n
You might recognise that the latter example is more like your traditional RPGs layout.

The tricky thing is is that many would argue the second example works, with the word 'works' used in the same sense as one might say the first example works. But I'd describe it as a Lateral Dislocate because all the pieces are disconnected. Sure, you can put them together, alot like lego, but they do not work as is. YET the thing is many gamers can so SEE a connection between each piece from looking at the dislocated pieces (kinda like a flicker fusion), they see a 'working' game right from the get go.

You then try and argue it doesn't work because what do you do after char gen and they'll say well you have an adventure, then you ask how do you do that, well they point at chapter 4 and so on.

It forms a kind of endless cycle where the person who thinks it works keeps seeing connections between the chapters. And in doing so, they are authoring a game just like the first example is - but a game they will insist actually exist there to begin with.

The other thing is the first example, the Serial Spine, shows how mechanics can utterly shape play. The thing with the Lateral Dislocate example, is that essentially it is very much a fiction first amalgamation. That's why alot of people have trouble imagining any rules as structuring their play, and quite rightly as their experience is that the chapter those rules are in might simply get skipped. In a Lateral Dislocate structure, what set of rules comes up next is really up for grabs. So the rules, kinda like a dislocated shoulder, can't really influence much - as soon as rules start controlling the fiction (in a way disliked by the person who chooses the next chapter), the choose of the next chapter makes sure to choose rules that will undermine the effects they didn't like (or choose no rules at all - the classic 'we spent the whole evening roleplaying and didn't roll once' responce or even just long period where the chooser will only let spoken fiction occur and no rule use (mollases play)).

Given all that, yeah, you try and talk about rules deciding play and people instantly think of a Lateral Dislocate structure and then quite rightly think that the rules can't control stuff (since they are so subbordinate to the chooser).

So I thought I'd give a contrasting example of the Serial Spine game - a game which has a mechanical spine that starts from the very start of play and takes you to the very end of play. Some newer RPG's have a mechanical spine extending to a game ending - Escape from tentacle city is one example.

Hopefully that raises some hope in ya'll that mechanics can actually decide the overall structure of play. And that the 'Lateral Dislocate' structure is not the only choice you can make in terms of RPG structural design.

Thank you.
\n
\n

    Callan: would you say that the two procedures you presented are both intended to produce the same play experience or are they meant to be different games? In other words, are you advocating for a more effective presentation of familiar ideas or are you advocating for a play style that would seem novel to a traditional RPG player?

    \n I would actually say the Lateral Dislocate example doesn't even qualify as a game. Let alone able to be the same experience.

    As I said, many gamers can't seem to help but see connections between the Lateral Dislocates disparate components and will insist they are there and 'it's playable'. I'm kind of waiting for that insistance to come up, to engage with it.

    What generally happens is that with the author of a Lateral Dislocate system their gameplay is rather consistant, and so rather like the serial spine. But when they go on to write a game, they are victims of legacy design and write up a Lateral Dislocate system.

    Now I grant that some people upon recieving the game, just by chance, might put together the disparate components into a game that is much like the authors play. But mostly on a 'monkeys might fly out of my butt' principle.

    The majority will, if they attempt to get play going, hash together the components in some way that 'seems to make sense' to them. They will then confuse their creation as a game that was already written down in the text (and worse, the text will encourage them to think this is the case). Indeed, some will think it 'makes so much sense' that it's incredibly wonderful - which is a kind of narcisism as they fall in love with their own design and of course it makes intimate sense to their brain, because it is a direct product of their brain! But they attribute it as being the creation of someone else. Which is a bit heartbreaking when you see designers/creative people who deny they are creative, that it's all 'in the game' and spend hours of their leasure time denying such.

    Or alternatively, instead of just laying the boot into Lateral Dislocate systems, I'm also pointing out how a mechanical spine can be set from the begining of play right to the end. And by it you can decide play - it doesn't just turn out however the group plays the game. You can take control as a designer. I think alot of people, used to Dislocate systems, have thrown their hands in the air at the idea of writing something which actually controls play. They've got the idea that nothing they write in a game can affect gameplay unless the GM lets it, so they've given up on the idea. So here's a morale boost in regards to taking control in game design!

    \n I agree 100% except that I think "game" is fuzzy enough to incorporate both of these approaches. After all, as you note, for the last 30 years we've called the second text a role-playing game (well...there's actually no rules for roleplaying in either game but you get the picture.) I can get picky about tons of semantic crap, but "game"? Nah, that's a big fuzzy word with tons of different things inside it.

    Yes, in example 2, what's happening is the players are actually assembling the game, doing design work of their own. This is why RPGs in this form are fun and worth playing. The form can even be pushed more in that direction while still remaining fun and awesome: GURPS, Universalis, etc. But players who don't want to do that won't have fun with RPGs in that form.

    I disagree that players of games like game 2 are in any way deluded about what they're doing to any significant degree. What do you think "let me tell you about my character" really means?

    That said, I see Apocalypse World as an example of a Serial Spine game that has decision making. You pick your moves/options from a list that are already provided for you by the designer so that when you choose them your play will be appropriate.

    \n JDCorley,

    Well, no definition survives contact with desire. And I think it's a bit global to say 'this is why RPG's are fun', but maybe it seemed like I was saying a global statement as well. Really, over many, many discussions I have really not encountered many gamers who will state 'what's happening is the players are actually assembling the game, doing design work of their own'. I really haven't - I'm honestly surprised here to hear you state that explicitly. Most seemed to deny such things even as I point at it explictly. Maybe I'm wrong in my observation and they all/99% of them got it over the years - I'd prefer that to be the case, to be honest.

    But at the very least, there's a big distinction between someone who explicitly acknowledges that they assemble the game Vs someone who thinks they 'just play the game'. The former I think is entirely viable and hey, have fun doing that! The latter is not viable - you might argue no one falls into that camp. And I'd say I'd like if that were the case.

    I still don't really know what "let me tell you about my character" means?


    Frozenwastes,

    Well, I'd actually describe getting procedure of play from tradition as a bug, not a feature. Yes, they'll use a tradition - which means they'll...play the same way they always do. That's what traditions are. A new game wont actually mean a new game, it'll be the same way they usually play, but with a different setting. Tradition will dominate the structure of play and new rules wont make a difference. Heck, as much as I admire the riddle of steel RPG, I played it once and entirely forgot to introduce players spiritual attributes - I was a prisoner of tradition and nothing stopped me from ignoring one of the main features of the game. One can try and bop me over the head to try and remember it, but that's just enforcing a Serial Spine (use SA's at so and so time, Callan!) without acknowledging it in textual form.


    Dreamofpeace,

    Well the SS version doesn't even mention 'adventure', I'll grant. You just...play.

    Otherwise I'm kind of being very generous to your traditional LD game, in that the 'adventure' section for a trad RPG is far more ambiguous than what I've described. Indeed, what traditional games even have an 'adventure' section? Yet what are alot of traditional RPG's about? Adventure! My examples might be bad in that I give more advice on how to actually have an adventure in the LD adventure game than a traditional RPG adventure game gives on how to have an adventure!

    You know, I am kinda coming up a blank - really most trad RPG's just don't have a section on how to run an adventure. Or atleast from my memory they don't. Even as they are about adventure, or so they say. Maybe an example will pop to mind for someone else?

    Anyway, in as much as I say the LD example doesn't actually do anything, I'd agree the adventure section/the 20k doesn't do the same thing as the SS 20k does. In the LD example the 20k just sits there, spinning in semantic space unless someone assembles it into the game they are playing as they build it.

    Myself, I think your question has highlighted another large hole in trad RPG's - if were doing adventures, how do we do that? Dunno.

    \n To answer the OP, I totally agree, except perhaps the remark of the game part. Something most (traditional) roleplaying games lack is telling how to play the game. How a game session should be played out. That's the important part of roleplaying games.

    When looking at other forms of games, like board games and card games, you got clear (and short) structures for how to play the game from start to end. In other words, board- and card games got a spinal structure. They sometimes got optional rules for changing how to play the game. Note however that rules are procedural structures, but structures doesn't have to be game mechanics. Following a list of structures isn't really considered game mechanics, but just as rules, it's still something you need to follow.

    In my first published game, I wanted to provide a game style. The GM advice in my book are the heart of the game, and the rules supported those advices. What happened was that only a few people read the advice, because they brought their own conventions on how to play roleplaying games into their reading. They didn't think they needed to read about how to play the game. So I got complaints that some of the game mechanics didn't work, because they used their own conventions.

    So I experimented and released another version of the game. It didn't have the traditional chapters. It just had Conventions, Character Creation and Adventure Creation. The Convention chapter had rules in it but it was a subdued by the text on how you should think to play the game. I got complaints that I had to many conventions, and one person even suggested me checking up AW to see how the writer could manage with fewer conventions. I did that, and I discovered that AW had as many conventions as my game.

    This made me think. People seem to be better at taking in rules than GM advice. In my fourth game, I still wanted to provide a game style. Rules however, are nothing more than structures, so I tried writing the whole game as a structure. A sequence of play that instructs people how to play my game. I don't know how it will turn out, because I haven't published it yet. It's something I'm looking forward to find out.

    So you should think in structures if you want to write a game, but most of all, describe the whole sequence of play and nothing more than that. Doing this is actually a form of "show, don't tell".

    \n Interesting point to be made here: I see an analogy between these two types of rules design, and between pre-made RPG adventures and the *World paradigm of designing Fronts.

    In the first, you have a set progression (in some way) of action, and deviating from that progression causes trouble. However, it is very good for producing a very specific type of story. Fiasco and Misspent Youth are both examples of this style of game.

    In the second, you have a bunch of elements that you build up, and the players' inertia helps you to determine how they all fall together. Burning Wheel is one excellent example of this style of game.

    Most games fall somewhere in the middle, and I think the concept you're looking for is the existence of an implicit narrative arc built into the game. The less of an implicit narrative arc there is, the more of a toolkit the game is, and the more it tends towards Lateral Dislocation.
    \n

    \nI completely agree. And I'd add that even if the goal is to make the play similar to how it's been done before, you should probably still have the actual procedures in the text rather than hoping the people import the right bits of tradition.

    Okay, now really in regards to my SS example, do any of them actually give an explicit number that leads to the end of the game, as well as a count method to get there (and a series of steps to follow in a loop till you do)?

    Or even in my LD example, do they even just have an explicit number and a notion of rolling a D6 to get toward it or something?

    I would agree virtually all RPG's waffle on about stuff. But no, I'd say it's actually ludicrous to say that all RPG's tell you how to go from the start of play to the end of the game.

    It sounds just like the 'I just play the game' illusion - except here it's 'I just follow the adventure designing advice' rather than admitting such advice has little to no influence on the adventure you design. As much as the Lateral Dislocate structure has little to no influence on which chapter you will enact. The advice is also in Lateral Dislocate structure - like a buffet, just pick and choose what you listen to, if any. And picking and choosing is fine, unless one also says 'oh, I just follow the advice', which isn't true if you pick and choose.

    I'm trying to think of an example where 'advice' could definately change adventure design, but that 'advice' could not just be called a rule. But I can't really think that's even possible? Do you have some examples of where adventure design advice stopped you from doing something/genuinely affected adventure design, but at the same time was still just advice and not a rule?


    CarpeGuitarrem,\n
    I think the concept you're looking for is the existence of an implicit narrative arc built into the game. The less of an implicit narrative arc there is, the more of a toolkit the game is, and the more it tends towards Lateral Dislocation.
    \nWell, one might even say: the more implicit that implicit narrative arc becomes, the more of a toolkit the game is.

    Ie, I'd kind of describe the implicitness as a problem.

    I don't argue that some RPG's probably rest on an implicit arc - the other day I was actually forming a post in my head about conflict resolution in relation to something like that.

    But I don't really see any RPG's just admit that explicitly in their text. It becomes too implicit. And because of that 'can't bear to say it' issue, it risks going from implicit to dissapearing entirely, the whole thing collapsing into Lateral Dislocate. I think Ron Edwards is probably in favour of the implicit narrative arc. I certainly don't agree if you don't point it out in some introductory text.

    It sounds just like the 'I just play the game' illusion - except here it's 'I just follow the adventure designing advice' rather than admitting such advice has little to no influence on the adventure you design.
    \nYes, within the context of the second type of game (your jargon is terrible by the by, I will never use it) the "how to make an adventure" chapters give you directions on how to make adventures, but not an explicit mechanical procedure, just like virtually everything in the second type of game. Within the context of the second type of game, this is exactly, exactly what must be done and which makes the second type of game fun to play.

    Also, that advice has overwhelming influence on the adventure you design. You will never convince me otherwise without a peer-reviewed statistical enterprise that carefully analyzed gaming groups and compared the adventures designed to the advice about how to design it.\n
    Do you have some examples of where adventure design advice stopped you from doing something/genuinely affected adventure design, but at the same time was still just advice and not a rule?
    \nYes, I was grouchily looking at D&D3 for a Birthright-a-like game but running into all kinds of problems with social situations and ruling situations. Then I read the first three paragraphs of the introduction where it reminded me that the game was about adventure and the job of the GM is to present challenges. That structure helped me toss some of the shit the game was not going to mechanically support and what was left was much stronger. In fact I found myself wishing I'd read it earlier. The advice would have solved a shitload of the problems I had with D&D3.

    Also, did you not see my huge "how to play Vampire 1ed" thread where everyone was shocked to learn that if the GMs had actually followed what the book said to do that most of their problems would not have occurred? Or is this evidence that advice doesn't actually do anything?

    I would put this question back to you: Imagine D&D3, printed as-is but with all DM instructions removed. Would the game be played the same?

    \n
    \n Callan, I don't think these two are as different as you think, at least with the examples you gave. The second isn't so much incomplete procedurally, but is (1) missing information (e.g. that monsters attack) and (2) actually different than the first (e.g. in the first game monsters only show up when you travel 1 km).

    What you've presented is more like a question of how to write a textbook. Do you write it so that it is good at explaining the concepts to someone new to them, or do you write something to be a good reference for someone who learned it once and remembers the basics, but needs a refresher on the details.

    I'll grant that what you're talking about is something thought to be a frustrating aspect of RPG rulebooks: that they are oddly presented not prescriptive about the procedures of play. However, as JDCorley points out, that the lack of prescription not actually be as true as we think.

    CarpeGuitarrem,\n
    I think the concept you're looking for is the existence of an implicit narrative arc built into the game. The less of an implicit narrative arc there is, the more of a toolkit the game is, and the more it tends towards Lateral Dislocation.
    \nWell, one might even say: the more implicit that implicit narrative arc becomes, the more of a toolkit the game is.

    Ie, I'd kind of describe the implicitness as a problem.

    I don't argue that some RPG's probably rest on an implicit arc - the other day I was actually forming a post in my head about conflict resolution in relation to something like that.

    But I don't really see any RPG's just admit that explicitly in their text. It becomes too implicit. And because of that 'can't bear to say it' issue, it risks going from implicit to dissapearing entirely, the whole thing collapsing into Lateral Dislocate. I think Ron Edwards is probably in favour of the implicit narrative arc. I certainly don't agree if you don't point it out in some introductory text.
    \nThere's a few I can think of, off the top of my head. Fiasco has a very obvious and explicit narrative arc, and so does Misspent Youth. There's My Life With Master, and Hollowpoint, as well. Generally speaking, each of these games says "this is what sort of story you're going to run". They're very tightly-written games, because they're intended to deliver a very specific experience.

    \n As a side note: I really dislike when examples are around combats, because 95 % of all examples in roleplaying discussions are about combat. \n
    Rickard,

    it's really interesting to watch the evolution involved with the history you describe (and that you kept at it and tried new approaches again and again!)

    I'm not sure what you mean by the following? Why do you need to follow them? Do you mean once you've called up, say, the combat rules, then you aught to do the steps of it (initiative, turn order, and so on?)\n
    Following a list of structures isn't really considered game mechanics, but just as rules, it's still something you need to follow.
    \nYou got instructions for a certain situation and you need to go through it to resolve it. It doesn't matter if it's a structure for how to frame scenes, on how to write your background, on how to create twists in the story or just how the characters are introduced.

    I can only think of two ways to deliver a structure: by a check list that you must go through (aka "procedures"), or by presenting a list that you must pick something of. I guess the sequence of play is nothing more that a structure on how to deliver structures. I've just read Kagematsu and that game got a structure each for courtship, confrontation and conclusion, and they are all sorted into a sequence of play.

    Sequence of play can also have structures that are looped or repeated. When you play AW and using moves, for example, the moves have the same structure. You don't really need to roll dice, all you need is to understand what makes a good situation.

    [edit, changed]
    Declare your intent. Pick one below.
    - the GM activates a counter-move.
    - you will succeed but with a twist. Either by partly succeeding or the GM activates a counter-move.
    - achieve your intent.
    [/edit]

    These moves (structures) are looped over and over again through the session. But like I said before, you can do this with all kinds of structures, not just game mechanics. Framing scenes is the most obvious example.

    (I normally don't like talking about rules, because that locks your mindset. I rather talk about structures.)

    CarpeGuitarrem,
    I don't own those particular games. It depends, they might be more explicit yet still no have an overall spine from the begining of play to the end. If they aren't SS, then they bank on the end users innate habit of applying a begining, middle and end to a session (or some kind of story like structure like that). I think that kind of works, in as much as, as Ron Edwards puts it, people are story creatures. Though it's interesting when someone with Edwards notion of story brain damage comes in contact with it (in a sort of car wreck sense). However, if the game designer has some weird story structure in mind for his game but does not put in a SS that supports that, then folk will just put in vanilla, regular old beginning, middle and end instead of that strange structure. As author you can't think 'Memento' and then expect people will just use that sort of story structure unless you enforce that.
    \nOh, they definitely do. They absolutely have a structure encoded into the rules, and it makes the game very worthwhile.

    Fiasco has a very strict structure--you start by making a Setup of characters in a volatile situation, play through Act I to entrench them in schemes, determine a Tilt that will shake everything up, play through Act II to bring everything to a head, and finally wind things down with the Aftermath, where characters' stories are unwound. All of those elements are explicitly required by the game. Scene resolution, too.

    Misspent Youth has a particular structure too: it's a progression of 7 specific scenes, each of which represents a different piece of the session's story arc. What each scene does (from Scene 1 to Scene 7) is specified in the rules, and each scene has a different specific role. They send the characters through an arc where things build, get really bad, and the characters ultimately get a shot at finally confronting the villains. That happens every session.

    Hollowpoint's structure is laid out as "Conflict, Conflict, Conflict" to achieve an initial objective, followed by another chain of Conflicts to achieve a secondary objective. It's a bit looser than the others, but there's still a spine there. There's also a very particular (but more organic) arc to it, by means of the economy of characters. When an Agent (one of the hyper-professionals attempting a mission) dies, or is otherwise removed from the game, and incorporates a complication that they prepared ahead of time (something designed to bring them into conflict with the mission objective), then they get to come in at the next level, as an Operative. They are considered to have "won the game" for that session. If they do that again as an Operative, they get to come in as a Handler. If they do it again as a Handler, they are considered to have "totally won", and the game is over. (It's actually a really elegantly-designed game, if you play it as intended. It's an engine built for a particular purpose.)

    I don't actually own My Life With Master, but from what I hear, it enforces a general plot arc where characters (evil minions) fail and fail again to defy their master's will, until eventually one of them snaps and goes to kill the master. Then the game ends.

    \n Callan: what's missing in your second game has nothing to do with the structure. Let me rewrite your two games to illustrate:

    Game 3 (rules of game 2, structure of game 1):

    1) Your character has 10 HP.
    2) Generally, roll 1d6 and travel that many kilometers.
    3) About 16% of the time monsters show up, each of which has 1 HP. If one does go to step 4, if not, go to step 5
    4) If your character and a monster fight each rolls 1d6. Whoever rolls higher wins. The loser loses 1 HP. Also the GM may grant a character +2 to hit, if it seems warranted.
    5) If you traveled a total of 20 kilometers, the adventure is complete. If not, go back to step 2.


    Game 4 (rules of game 1, structure of game 2):

    Chapter 1: Character Generation
    You have a fictional character represented by 10 HP.

    Chapter 2: Combat
    When your character fights a monster, each rolls 1D6 vs each other. If your roll is equal to or higher than the monsters roll, you beat it and can continue. If the monsters roll is higher, you lose 1 HP. If you lose all 10 HP, you lose the game and the game ends. Keep rolling until either you or the monster is beaten. You may describe a fictional account of your attack on the monster.

    Chapter 3: Monsters
    Whenever you travel 1km at a time a monster appears and fights you.

    Chapter 4: Movement
    Roll 1D6 - this is how far you traveled in kilometeres. Record the amount.

    Chapter 5: Adventures
    You have a travel distance of 20 kilometers you need to make to complete this game. If you make it to 20km, you win.

    Chapter 6: GM advice
    If you (the GM) feel moved to do so, you may grant the player +2 added upon your dice roll. If the player still doesn't beat the monster, he continues to use the +2 bonus until the monster is defeated.


    Notice that game 3 is incomplete (how do you know when monsters show up? do they always attack?) even though it's presented as procedure, while game 4 is complete, even though it's disjointed.

    \n Callan,

    I'm curious if you've ever read the Solar System booklet, by Eero Tuovinen? (Or Dogs in the Vineyard, as another example, if you're not familiar with that one, although I don't think it's as thorough about how to use the rules in play.)

    I ask because it is at once, a) a good teaching text, and b) a "linear dislocate" structure.

    In other words, how do you feel about a game which, mechanically, is just like the loose, vague, structureless design you're describing here, but is really, really good at telling you how and when to use all those parts in play?

    If you have no idea what I'm talking about, I suppose I could try to write one of these mini-games like we have in this thread as illustration.

    But those games would be the polar opposite of something like Fiasco (very clear "spine" and serial structure, but a lot of vagueness in how to execute the actual steps, whereas I'm describing here something more like a lateral dislocate structure, but accompanied with a great clarity of intent about when to use each of the tools in the box and how).

    \n\nMcdaldno \n\n\n
    \n
    \n\nMarch 2013 \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n
    Timonkey,

    Game 4 isn't complete. Roll 1D6 when?
    \nAfter creating a character, and whenever you've got no monsters to fight, you travel. Roll 1D6 - this is how far you travel in kilometers. Record the amount after traveling it.

    ***

    I really appreciated the two original terms (serial spine and lateral dislocate), but then found the argument that's followed them to be weird and silly. So now I've lost interest in the two terms.

    ***

    Design games that are as complete as possible.
    Design games that people are reliably able to play and enjoy.
    Design concise texts that illuminate procedures and connections.
    Don't design crappy roleplaying game texts.
    Roleplaying game texts are crappy when they fail to teach players how to connect the rules within them.
    Highly procedural games are easier to teach, all other things being equal.
    Highly procedural texts tend to create a homogenized understanding of "what play should look like."
    A homogenized understanding of "what play should look like" is often a goal, though isn't the only valid goal.
    Game texts can and always do utilize some degree of player instinct.
    Writers should try to be aware of the procedural gaps that rely on player instinct.
    Story games are allowed to have procedural gaps.
    Fiasco has them, Apocalypse World has them, all games have them.
    Advice and rules exist on the same continuum in roleplaying game texts.
    Advice shapes how people engage the rules.
    People sometimes reject advice.
    People also sometimes reject rules.
    I'm out.

    \n
    I'm curious if you've ever read the Solar System booklet, by Eero Tuovinen? (Or Dogs in the Vineyard, as another example, if you're not familiar with that one, although I don't think it's as thorough about how to use the rules in play.)
    \nI was actually going to post about those two as examples that did have clear procedures. I'll limit myself to Solar System as its the one I've read and played the most recently.

    In Solar System, you come up with a setting at the table with a group, then you drill down to focal points, come up with what types characters are appropriate, then you start making them. You open up with a heroic scene or iconic vision of the character that communicates the most important thing about her. Then talk about what got her to that place. And so on, generating the stats as you go. Then you carry on into secrets and Keys.

    The Story Guide then has a set procedure of doing an introductory scene for each character and from then on, you are on a circuit of play you pretty much never leave. The text even has a flow chart for you to use. When conflict comes up you also a have a step by step procedure, including escalating into extended conflict, changing stakes midstream and exiting into narration and then you're back in another box on the flow chart (either refreshment or consequences).\n
    In other words, how do you feel about a game which, mechanically, is just like the loose, vague, structureless design you're describing here, but is really, really good at telling you how and when to use all those parts in play?
    \nI could see it being taken that way. I didn't see what Eero wrote as being some suggestions that you might follow maybe, but talking about the actual procedures you go through at the table. Maybe I got what Eero was trying to do wrong and took the text too literally.
    \n
    \n
    \n
    \n

    \n
    I didn't see what Eero wrote as being some suggestions that you might follow maybe, but talking about the actual procedures you go through at the table.
    \nQuite right, that's how I see it, too. The difference is that the various rules and procedures can be triggered and/or applied in lots of different ways. The art of playing the game is largely in deciding when--for example--you call for an Ability Check. Whereas in a "serial spine" game, there's always an unambiguous next step (like in Fiasco).

    \n Joe: thanks for the fix.

    Callan: My point is that a disjointed presented isn't fundamentally missing procedure. It may be more work, it may be harder to learn from, and rules holes may be less obvious, but it's not fundamental to the presentation format.

    Should we have texts that are procedural? Probably. I've been considering doing a 1 page D&D hack, where the DM rules are a flowchart. But it's hard to present a complex game with all the rules in the order you use them. Even board games don't do that most of the time. I just got Thunderbolt Apache Leader, and that actually does have all the rules in order (actually, when they first become relevant, sometimes you have to go backwards to reread some details that didn't have quite enough context yet for their implications to be clear) but that's a one player game and not the norm. For most games I think this would be awkward, because the step where you pick weapons for your aircraft takes several pages, because it has to explain all the weapons.

    Mouse Guard seems a relevant RPG text. I remember it doing a good job of presenting the flow of play in layers, first in an overview of overall play, then going into detail on each section (e.g. Players Turn, GM's Turn), while also pointing to where the details are for each step. I think this represents a good balance between extremes.

    I just went through a bunch of old Game books (BESM 1st Ed, DC Heroes 3rd ed, TSR Conan (1985), Macho Women with Guns 1st Ed.).     I do feel like even the worst written RPGs are a "game." "Game" is such a broad term that you have to really stretch to say something is not a game.
    As to writing style, I have to say, any effort to improve the writing of RPG rule books. This looks like a great post describing a broken writing style. Too many times an author makes assumptions about the readers understanding, traditions or what was actually conveyed tot he reader. This example is something that any RPG designer can benefit from, thanks!

    \n Ah,
    I caught up with this thread, so the issue has more to do with disallowing player actions than presentation?
    This is a very interesting discussion to me. I think that even the most strongly worded rules requirements can and will be disregarded by GMs.
    I feel like part of the issue with writing these sorts of provisions is that there are large quantities of game play that are unknown to the designer. Which characters are in play, what the NPCs look/act like are entirely customizable. And "Story Game" phenomenon makes such writing even more difficult. For instance take a game like Solar System, where the characters and antagonists are completely unknown. But when the game designer can't make any assumptions about the PC, NPCs, setting or consequences of actions (like in PTA), it creates a challenge to write verbiage that clarifies the story arc/progression in a manner that is not confusing when used in actual play.
    You can write for that, but it requires a significant amount of wordsmithing. Ultimately, it is an effort that pays off though.

    As far as judging the quality of a design by what it does not allow, that just seems as broken as judging a design by what dice you need to play it. Sure, it is a factor, but it is not a good gauge of quality. Say yes or roll the dice is a good example of this. It forbids the GM from saying "No." While this was a novel concept at the time, this rule if taken too literally could ruin that game as much as when it is ignored.

    This is a great discussion though, thanks for starting this thread.
    Dave M

    I think in game 4 there's more than one interpretive option a person might guess in regard to when to roll 1D6 (if at all), which leads to something other than game 1 being played (remember, you have the benefit of having game 1 in front of you.     It just doesn't seem viable.

    \n Callan: I thought Mcdaldno's fix to game 4 was sufficient, but can you see that we at least could make it complete?

    Although, your comment about your "Lateral Dislocate" being like object-oriented programming without calling the object makes me think that by LD you mean game text that is both disjointed and incomplete. Is that a fair assessment? If so, I think you made a valid observation, but it really just boils down to: incomplete games are bad. My point is that a text can be disjointed but not incomplete.

    Actually, I now think that disjointed is even a bad term for it, as it implies incoherent. That need not be the case. Breaking up the game into pieces allows you to learn parts of it at a time, and understand the whole much earlier. Abstraction is very powerful.

    \n
    \n Here's a potentially interesting quote from an old thread, by @Emily_Care:\n
    And to stop the threadjacking, Eppy Ravachol has a standard rant that may address Gabor's issues. He calls it the Archipelago and the River Delta metaphor. Archipelago play (as distinct from playing Matthijs' Archipelago specifically) deals with navigating through the ocean of free play, and touching down on mechanics when the mood and moment are right. Hopping from island to island, there are a plethora of rules that can be invoked, and they are (when things are working correctly) when it is both right and desired. There's a huge amount of room for Rule Zero here. It's raised event to an aesthetic philosophy, which may be how it was intended all the time (but can be used to cover a world of errors). A River Delta game is one where the rules focus your play like the river banks do. You are not left to wonder where you should be headed, the rules hand you off from free play in the water to the next set of procedures.

    Now the River Delta is likely to be very dependable. You're less liable to get lost, or hit uncharted rocks in the water. But it's a completely different experience, and may be suited to different skills. Playing a primarily free-play game you are susceptible to the storms of player discontent, or may let you be beached on the shoals of GM burnout. The River Delta makes it much easier for anyone to pick up the role of GM/navigator. And this kind of structure is well suited to more distributed, multi-GM play. If there is not just one hand at the wheel, then having rules for how the many hands work together makes sense.
    \n

    \n Timonkey,

    If chapter 4 of your disjointed game included the text 'This is how you start play: ' before the rest of the text in that chapter, I'd agree on the disjointed notion. I wouldn't see much point in having it that way, but I would agree on it just being disjointed. Currently though the disjointedness seems a distraction for how it also does not describe a certain piece of procedure.


    Paul,

    Without being held to particulars of the analogy, yeah, River Delta = Serial Spine, Archipelago = Lateral Dislocate. Note: Though Emily might be refering to something like CarpeGuitarrem was with the River Delta idea, where there isn't an explicit spine and the game instead hangs off an implicit story structure (ie, the user has a notion of begining, middle and end). In which case see my conversations with CarpeGuitarrem - I wouldn't say that is serial spine, but its kinda like a close relative.

    I think it doesn't go into the details of how Achipelago/Lateral Dislocate's enaction of rules can simply cancel each other out (eg, GM chooses the fight 'island'. but right after he chooses the resting 'island', which then negates any mechanical effect the fight has...making the fight a pointless time waster if you're not just interested in a reenactment/simulationist experience), but I guess that would have complicated the distinction.

      \n Hmm, I see the point Eppy was making more about there being discretion in the use of rules (or at least mechanics). For example, contrast Apocalypse World, where the Moves are always triggered by very specific fictional situations, so there is no choice of whether or not to use them, with Burning Wheel, where for any character action the GM could Say Yes, call for a Test, or break out one of the subsystems like Duel of Wits. There's some advice for when to use each, but it's ultimately left as a choice for those playing the game, and involves some learning how to make that choice.

      \nFor the sake of my mechanical tea and sympathy thread, I'll quickly say 'fiction' triggers anything as much as the player characters trigger anything. Which is to say, something that does not exist obviously triggers nothing.       \n Callan, I don't understand the point you're trying to make, and reading the first post of that thread didn't help.

      I can see your perspective that the fiction doesn't exist as anything other than a creation of the players, so it's the players that ultimately determine when the Moves come up.       \n Yes! Callan S, I\u2019ve been having this exact same thought (that you expressed in the OP) for quite a while!
      It\u2019s not about a linear play experience\u2014likea series of pre-prepped combat encounters\u2014it\u2019s about a step by step presented rules layout, versus the more \u201cencyclopedic\u201d collection of loose rules and game data that is in most RPG books today.

      Many things are in tables that are like \xbbok, these are the various saving throw levels for clerics\xab etc. Instead of a very straight forward step-by-step\u2013approach that still end up being isomorphic to the same game rules, just presented as instructions rather than a collection of facts.

      Especially for the GM I think there\u2019s a huge lack of rules.

      Personally, I want to learn how to DM/GM a very open, sandboxy game and it\u2019s a real challenge to try to piece that together from gamebooks, and I\u2019m resorting to some OSR blogs that list various, often contrasting, methods on how to lay out a hex crawl world. (And I\u2019ve gotten some advice via email, which, in case they read this, I am not ungrateful for.)
      \n

      \n\nCallan_S \n\n\n

      \n timonkey, maybe my understanding of the mechanics from you description isn't up to scratch. From the sound of it, the players speak fiction and the GM, upon hearing it, may be moved to activate one of the AW moves. The pivotal issue is whether players just mechanically choose their move, or have to speak fiction that they hope fits/moves the GM to choose the move they wanted to activate. What I say applies to the latter. I'm not sure I understand you fully, but you seem to ignore the subjective element involved when you say 'Unlike some other games, once the fiction is set, there is no choice of mechanics.'. As some people like to cry out, were not robots - even if the mechanic to use after hearing the fiction seems obvious, it's the result of ones brain evaluating a series of ambiguous elements (we don't evaluate things like a robot) subjectively.

      Really it's alot like how in the past people thought the sun revolved around the earth - because it just appears to! It might seem what mechanic is used next revolves around the fiction, but actually what mechanic is used next revolves around the GM.

      Though there are other mechanics (like the player chooses the mechanic after they give some fiction that ties to it, for example), so maybe another type of mechanic is used in AW?


      2097, as far as I understand you, absolutely yes! Yes, you're actually bombarded with dozens or even hundreds of options, when it comes to even the first step of your hex crawl world. There's actually a perverse perceptivity/difficulty curve involved - the more you're aware of all the options, the harder it is to piece something together. While if ones perception can't actually see as many of the options and only sees a handful (or only one, even!!), it becomes easy to piece together (though along with it, far more rigid).

      I think you're at the high perception and with it high difficulty end of the curve where you can see a vast range of options - don't let others say 'it's easy'. It's really not a fair comparison between you and them. When they can see far fewer options, of course it's easier to choose from those options.

      I'm mostly saying that because I sense a workload on you and a sense that you think there's some extra trick to it you're pursuing and I want to ease the former and dispell the latter.

      And I love how you say 'often contrasting methods' - you're far more diplomatic than I am!
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\n2097 \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nMarch 2013 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      a sense that you think there's some extra trick to it you're pursuing
      \nWell, yes, but the trick that I\u2019m pursuing is to try to rewrite their declarative (\u201cLateral Dislocate\u201d) descriptions into a prodecural (\u201cSerial Spine\u201d) style. So that I have a checklist (\u201cdecide biotopes per hex using this-or-that method, draw borders surrounding regions of so-and-so\u2013many hexes\u2026\u201d) that I then can follow everytime I want to start a new sandbox. Coming from programming, there are also other approaches to try (OO, functional\u2026)

      I think it is a good thing if game designers write their games in a procedural/Serial Spine style.

      Sometimes rewriting declarative/LD games into a procedural style can show just how overwhelming a game is.
      Have you seen this?
      http://free.7host07.com/spdesign/files\\Combat_Chart_bicolored.pdf
      It\u2019s a rewrite of GURPS 4e combat (just combat) into procedural style.

      A good case could be made that the declarative/Lateral Dislocate style would be easier to approach. You start with a simple core, and then as you gain profeciency with the rules you add in subsystems. \u201cHmm, we use automatic fire\u2026 let\u2019s look it up\u2026 ok, it works like this-or-that\u201d. As you get to \u201cknow\u201d how the combat \u201cworks\u201d it becomes more and more detailed without having to learn something as jawdroppingly heavy as the procedural chart I linked to above.

      But, for me, the vagueness and \u201chow do I get started\u201d-ness that is the original GURPS books is just as bad. It has the information but it\u2019s distributed in an encyclopedic test that I need to gather clues from about how to play.


      Sometimes a rewrite to a procedural style shows that huge swaths of the game is either missing, or specific to a style that\u2019s been hard for me to pull off well. I agree completely with you that what is commonly called \u201cadventure design\u201d is either missing completely (as in, for example, McWoD) or, in say Trail of Cthulhu, a collection of setpieces and combat/clue-gathering encounters that reveals a playstyle that is less \u201cplay to find out what happens\u201d and more \u201clet the players reveal what the GM has planned\u201d that I would personally like. (A question of subjective taste, of course.)

      Actually, this is the main source of my newfound interest in D&D\u2014that the prep is place and random tables, not \u201cscenes\u201d or \u201cplot\u201d or \u201cthings that will happen\u201d, but not just GM winging it either.

      timonkey, maybe my understanding of the mechanics from you description isn't up to scratch. From the sound of it, the players speak fiction and the GM, upon hearing it, may be moved to activate one of the AW moves.
      \nNo, the GM has no discretion as to whether to activate a move or not. Here I'm not talking about MC/GM moves, I'm talking about player moves. Let's look at a Dungeon World Move (because they're more in plain english):

      Defend
      When you stand in defense of a person, item, or location under attack, [mechanics].

      The players control their characters and the GM everything else. Once the GM establishes that there is an Ogre about to stomp on a child, and then a player decides to move their character between them to defend the child, the Defend move kicks in. The situation is as outlined in the Move, so it kicks in.

      I've read through your Mechanical Tea & Sympathy thread, and I disagree with most of what you said there (and also admit to understanding almost none of it until someone else recapitulated it) . I've seen the "mother may I" situation in dysfunctional D&D3 play (e.g. why won't you just let me roll Diplomacy?), but that isn't going to happen in all games. You talked about the GM having the final say about what's in the fiction, but why and how? Are you saying that in the situation above the GM can just say, "no, you don't move to protect the child"?

      Also, something that might be causing a disconnect: when I say fiction with regards to RPGs, I really mean is shared imagined space (which I've seen written as SIS), which is the set of imagined events that exists in all of the players' minds (including the GM). If it hasn't been properly articulated, it doesn't count, and needs to be cleared up, but this is an issue of communication and not one that requires additional layers of authority. We already have rules about who has authority to contribute in what ways to the fiction/shared imagined space.

      Sometimes rewriting declarative/LD games into a procedural style can show just how overwhelming a game is.
      Have you seen this?
      http://free.7host07.com/spdesign/files\\Combat_Chart_bicolored.pdf
      It\u2019s a rewrite of GURPS 4e combat (just combat) into procedural style.
      \nThat overwhelmingness is why "LD" games exist, though. Here's where the procedural vs. object-oriented programming analogy really shines. If you rewrote every bit of software on the market as a procedural program, it would be ludicrous. And that's because humans don't understand things on a procedural basis. We understand things as an abstract collection of objects (with characteristics) that interact with one another, forming specific connections.

      Procedural writing is just a way that organizes things for us, but only as a means to understanding the objects intuitively.

      \n More accurate variations: "I thought you were over by the bookshelves. Isn't that on the other side of the room?" / "Aren't you an insubstantial ghost? How does leaping to the child's defence really help?" / "Aren't you all wrapped up with the displacer beast's tentacles? How do you pull out of those in time to jump to the child's defence?" Bonus, this GM is following the rules more, unlike the other one, by being a fan of the players and asking questions and using the answers to clarify the fiction. The GM isn't the final arbiter of the fiction, but if the fiction isn't clear it's definitely the GM's job to ask questions to clarify it. If someone is doing something that sounds invalid based on previously established fiction, then obviously the fiction isn't clear to everyone and it's time again to ask more questions until everyone is on the same page.

      I'll say it again though: the GM is not the final arbiter on the fiction or the moves triggered by it; it is the GM's job to make suite the fiction is clear to everyone; when the fiction is not clear to everyone, ask questions to clarify the situation.

      Here's where the procedural vs. object-oriented programming analogy really shines. If you rewrote every bit of software on the market as a procedural program, it would be ludicrous. And that's because humans don't understand things on a procedural basis. We understand things as an abstract collection of objects (with characteristics) that interact with one another, forming specific connections.

      Procedural writing is just a way that organizes things for us, but only as a means to understanding the objects intuitively.
      \nI agree.

      I can't be the only person around here who needs to have that kind of understanding in order to really feel like I know a game well. How proceduralized the text is can affect how long it takes me to wrap my head around a game, but in general I'm always going to want to have a full command of the step-by-step part and the Big Picture overview. Only having one or the other just doesn't cut it.

      (I will say that in games with a lot of moving parts, I seem to get to that level of understanding faster when the book is giving me the less-proceduralized approach and I'm the one working out the step-by-step instructions, rather than the other way around. Something about having everything split up into neatly labeled, bite-sized sections makes getting a handle on a game go much more quickly for me...and makes for a better/easier reference book to use during a game session, too. Other people's mileage no doubt varies on that.)

      \n Timonkey,

      You're treating 'mother may I' as if its only in dysfunctional games. This is the same sort of reception the Lumpley principle got - people said 'well, maybe in some (bad) games, but in mine we just go and do X and Y in the fiction'. Mother may I is in every single session of roleplay you've ever played. When it gets to a subtle, sympathetic level, it becomes almost invisible, to the extent you think it only exists in 'why won't you just let me roll Diplomacy?' examples. No, that's just where MMI becomes more visible.

      The issue is, you can't see the subjective evaluation occuring. \n
      Once the GM establishes that there is an Ogre about to stomp on a child, and then a player decides to move their character between them to defend the child, the Defend move kicks in.
      \nI mean, this isn't an example of play at all. Who at the table says 'and then a player decides to move'??? It's not that way at all - you give a single narrative of the event (where of course you don't say anything to contradict yourself in the narrative). When you have more than one narrative/person, contradictions easily come up. It's several people at the table, and several narratives (ie, individual RL lives) going on.

      If you could give the example with what each person actually says (what the GM says, what the player says) then we get closer to the subjective evaluation that occurs (by someone at the table).

      Right now it's you who are saying the character was definately moved to defend the child. Right now were simply working with your single narrative and as much as you're not going to contradict yourself, your narrative makes sense. But when we work with two narratives (because as Ralph of universalis says, there is no SIS, just a series of seperate imagined spaces/narratives), it's not so simple.

      I thought this would hook conversation over, almost off topic - but at the same time it's intimately tied with it's sister thread (Mechanical Tea and GM sympathy)

      the GM is not the final arbiter on the fiction or the moves triggered by it; it is the GM's job to make suite the fiction is clear to everyone; when the fiction is not clear to everyone, ask questions to clarify the situation.
      \nYou have real people at the table - and real people don't always agree/they argue. Repeatedly going into extra detail on the fiction wont matter if the other person remains unconvinced.

      I know the classic gamer responce is that no one at the table is going to think in a contradictory way to anyone else ('they just need a bit more fiction cleared up, that is all...'), or anyone who does must only do so to be a douche or something. The echo chamber answer.

      Maybe I'm the odd one out (though Ron Edwards seems to be a contradictory thinker) and roleplay from the start has been all about play where no one lets themselves remain unconvinced. Or maybe roleplay culture is different in Australia. Just that pretty much everyone one I've gamed with or even ever met tend to have their own strident thoughts. I can't imagine them quashing them out side of play, let alone inside of play just to 'keep the game going'. They are the values that matter to them - matter more than a game. And so there's a need for a final say (which means they don't have to quash them or agree, they just have to accept the final says declaration on what were doing next in play).

      \n I understand shared imagined space (SIS) in terms of set theory. It's the set of imagined stuff (regarding the game at hand) which is common to all players. That certainly exists, it's just hard to say exactly what it is, because we can't read all the players' minds. However, sometimes when someone contributes something that doesn't make sense to another player, because it doesn't fit with what they've imagined, it becomes obvious that the SIS isn't complete enough for play to continue. At that point, players need to basically rewind a little bit to build up the SIS appropriately.

      For example:

      GM: An Ogre comes into the room and looks to be about to stomp on a child.
      Player (Chris): I move to protect him.
      GM: You can't move to protect the child, you're too far away.
      Chris: What? I'm standing right next to him.
      GM: I thought you were over by the bookshelves.
      Chris: Yeah.
      GM: (sketches room or points or something) So then he's here, and you're there.
      Chris: Oh. Shit. Then I guess I ...

      I will claim that Mother May I when applied to rules, meaning that you have to get the GM's permission to use the rules, is always dysfunctional play. I invite examples to the contrary.

      Mother May I in terms of getting the GM to think you have a good plan, and thus deserve success or mechanical bonuses, within the bounds of the rules, can be very good play, if understood and/or articulated clearly. Freeform works off of that, and I think a lot of old school D&D.

      CarpeGuitarrem,
      No one understands the very procedural game of chess?
      \nNot everyone plays chess procedurally. Sure, some chess players are all about memorizing sequences of moves so that they can counter X with Y, because they've seen a position before...but actual chess tactics rely on an understanding of the role played by each piece, of the purpose of controlling space and setting up offenses (and defenses), of learning a few simple combinations and how to apply them.

      In fact, you could only play chess 100% procedurally if you had the ability of a computer to retrieve chess positions from historical games (or to look ahead therein). Learning what a fork is, or a discovered check, or understanding why a zugzwang happens, those are pieces of the art of chess, and that's exactly how Lateral Dislocate works. You learn pieces, and then you figure out how to stitch them together through experience.

      It takes baby steps, but eventually you figure out how to look at a situation and recognize that Tool X which you've learned how to use is perfect, or maybe Tool Y is the right one because it'll set you up in the long run. But that's the actual art of chess. In fact, I'd argue that chess is deceptively non-procedural.

      Timonkey suggested that. I don't think I agree - I think the main issue in that gurps flow chart you wrote that if you have four players, then you are going to be going through that flowchart four times, at the same time!!!
      \nSequentially, not at the same time. One issue is that this whole four-page chart is supposed to represent one players\u2019 turn, and once you\u2019ve gone around the table, including for the monsters, one second has passed in the game world.
      Whereas if you start from the core of \u201c3d6 must be rolled under the attackers skill, 3d6 over the passive defense, and Xd6+Y must be calculated as damage (vs armor, hitpoints etc)\u201d (if you have three different styles of d6 you can roll them all together) it can very well take just a few seconds in gaming time and is better suited to represent one second in the game world. Then if someone has comprehensive Lateral Dislocate knowledge, they might say things like \u201cOh, my Karate guy can do this special Frobnicate Xyzzy here\u201d and do it.
      But I\u2019m with you. The declarative/LD style is hard to learn and a nagging feeling that \u201cI\u2019m missing something\u201d will never leave unless I go through a procedural rewrite.
      My original point by linking to this GURPS chart was to show how a declarative style might look simple in the eyes of the game writers, but when converted to a concrete method of play, ether explicitly through a rewrite like this, or implicitly through player comprehension / learning of the game\u2019s rules, become extremely complex.\n
      Also it doesn't seem comprehensive - check if the sides detect each other? How?
      \nYes, many parts of the chart reference other parts of GURPS.
      \n
      That overwhelmingness is why "LD" games exist, though. Here's where the procedural vs. object-oriented programming analogy really shines. If you rewrote every bit of software on the market as a procedural program, it would be ludicrous. And that's because humans don't understand things on a procedural basis. We understand things as an abstract collection of objects (with characteristics) that interact with one another, forming specific connections.

      Procedural writing is just a way that organizes things for us, but only as a means to understanding the objects intuitively.
      \nCarpeGuitarrem, it\u2019s clear that you get what I mean, but let\u2019s go a bit further.
      As a writer, the procedural style is horrible. I never program that way. I dig the functional style but for some domains declarative works better for me.

      But in order for a processor to use the program, it needs to either be compiled into procedural form (opcodes in machine language or a bytecode middle step) or it needs to be interpreted by an interpreter that knows how to follow the instructions of the language.
      In the analogy,
      in order for a player to use the game, it needs to be either restated into procedural form (through explicit rewrites/flowcharts, or internalized and organized in the brain) or the game text needs to be followed by someone who is through tradition very familiar with how to work with that type of rules text.

      And, just like how profiling a compiled and running process can reveal problems in the underlying code, regardless of language paradigm, a restatement of the LD/declarative style language into the procedural form can reveal issues of complexity or holes.

      Many humans are \u201cprocessors\u201d rather than writers. Have you noticed, for example, that Wikipedia is great with its declarative style, but \u201cquestion-asking sites\u201d have popped up in the thousands because they\u2019re easier to understand?

      Callan\u2019s example in the OP rang very true for me. In the declarative example we had stuff like \u201cmonsters appear 16% of the time\u201d. Huh? I get, as a seasoned gamer, that there are many methods I can use to determine a 16% chance (doubles on 2d6, percentile dice, 11+ on d12 etc), but it\u2019s not clear WHEN to use those methods. If, say, my parents would read a rule like that, they\u2019d have no idea what to do with it. In the procedural example, on the other hand, we had clear instructions on what triggered a monster appearance. Bliss!



      (Now this next bit is fairly off-topic to the subject of procedural vs declarative.)\n
      gold=xp
      \nWe do the same. We explore a huge dungeon that the DM has bought in a book, we have gold=xp to drive the story and we scout out various areas of this dungeon. At any given time it feels like we have plenty of options. If I get to DM, I want to add a hex \u201coverworld\u201d to bring even more choice to the game.
      In the past I always dismissed D&D because I would look at the things that other games did\u2014character generation, fighting\u2014and I would conclude that the way D&D did those things was no big whoop. But now my eyes have opened to the things that D&D does that these other games didn\u2019t do at all\u2014present a sense of verisimillitude, things to really interact with, explore and change\u2014and now all the other games feel like they are just half games.

      \n 1. Assign one player to be black and one to be white, by a method outside of these rules.
      2. Seat the players opposite one another.
      3. Turn the chess board so that if it has coordinates, the square A1 is at the left hand of the white player, and if it does not, a dark square is at the left hand of the white player.
      4. Set up the pieces like this [omitted from this example].
      5. Alternating turns, starting with the white player, the players
      [followed by instructions for a single turn, including restrictions such as when you can\u2019t castle, en-passant or end your turn with your king in check]
      6. The game ends when it is not possible to make a move [and/or various repetition rules, 50-move rules, stalemate rules, agreed draw etc].

      Even with a strict procedural \u201cserial spine\u201d the gameplay itself is wide open. There\u2019s no strict procedures for how to choose the best move. (Of course, Botvinnik, Steinitz and others have created attempts of such for themselves and their students.)
      That\u2019s why the term \u201cserial spine\u201d didn\u2019t appeal to me\u2014I love a serial rules presentation but dislike serial gameplay.
      I want to understand the rules of the game\u2014ergo, remove confusion around things like "monsters appear 16% of the time\u201d but I still want a wide open field of choices on how to best play the game.

      CarpeGuitarrem,

      It's a curious perspective you take - but it appears 2097 is taking it too? Granted my first example of a serial spine is very simple, but I'm not sure why you treat serial rule sequences as serial gameplay?

      A serial sequence can offer you 100 choices. Sequentially you can go onto one of the choices which, lets say, offers another 100 choices. Sequentially going onto one of these offers a further 100 choices.

      That's serial spine. That's how chess is played - every piece offers choices (lots of choices - not sure how many, on each of your (serial) turns, fewer choices as you lose pieces). Everyone plays chess in a serial spine manner. Not everyone chooses the same choices that the serial spine offers, throughout it's spine.

      Carpe, you're just refering to choosing from amongst the many choices a chess game provides in it's serial spine format. You're not describing a lateral dislocate AT ALL! You are just describing play in a serial spine game.

      This is probably the big hurdle to get ones head around, if you think that in a traditional game as GM if you choose to start a combat, that it's like choosing to move a pawn forward in chess. No, not at all. If you think of a traditional RPG as actually about a half dozen seperate boardgames (one of them being combat), then what you are doing is like choosing to play chess to begin with, when you choose to activate the combat rules.

      The lateral dislocate choice is like "Which boardgame will I play tonight?", it's not like "Which chess piece will I move?" (which is serial spine)

      Anyway, if I play chess, then after I play poker, it's not playing some chessy/pokery overall game. It's just one game after another. Dislocated from each other.

      You might not buy the hypothesis, fair enough, but try and describe in your own words how you understand the idea. It's basically 'one boardgame after another' vs 'one choice after another, presented by just the one boardgame'. Even if you don't agree, how would you describe that in your own words?

      However, I made the aside that I think the adjective \u201cserial\u201d has a very negative connotation (from being applied to serial, railroady type gameplay), and that\u2019s why I prefer the word \u201cprocedural\u201d over \u201cSerial Spine\u201d. Another reason is the established language in programming, a discipline similar to rules writing but with a few very important differences.\n
      A serial sequence can offer you 100 choices. Sequentially you can go onto one of the choices which, lets say, offers another 100 choices. Sequentially going onto one of these offers a further 100 choices.

      I think that roleplaying rules should, at least at some point during their development, be stated in procedural format\u2014what you, Callan, call Serial Spine format. (And I\u2019m fine with \u201cend game\u201d being just \u201cwhen you grow tired of playing\u201d.)

      And the way that they are finally presented to the reader should be in a much more practical format than the encyclopedic/declarative/Lateral Dislocate style that is common today.

      Whether that format is Serial Spine or something else is up for grabs but as it stands, I don\u2019t think many games are particularly usable.

      timonkey,\n
      I will claim that Mother May I when applied to rules, meaning that you have to get the GM's permission to use the rules, is always dysfunctional play. I invite examples to the contrary.

      Mother May I in terms of getting the GM to think you have a good plan, and thus deserve success or mechanical bonuses, within the bounds of the rules, can be very good play, if understood and/or articulated clearly. Freeform works off of that, and I think a lot of old school D&D.
      \nI'm boggled - to me you just described the same thing twice ('get GM's permission to use rules'/'GM thinks you deserve mechanical bonuses' is the exact same thing).

      How are you distinguishing them?

      \nOkay, here's the difference. Let's look at a couple rules from D&D3. The first rule is that the Diplomacy skill can be used to convince NPCs to do stuff. The second rule is that the GM may grant players bonuses or penalties for situations of advantage or disadvantage. If the GM decides whether or not I get to roll my Diplomacy skill, I have to get his permission to use the rules. When the GM decides whether or not I get a bonus, we are following the rule because the rule says that that's what he's supposed to do.

      A serial sequence can offer you 100 choices. Sequentially you can go onto one of the choices which, lets say, offers another 100 choices. Sequentially going onto one of these offers a further 100 choices.

      That's serial spine. That's how chess is played - every piece offers choices (lots of choices - not sure how many, on each of your (serial) turns, fewer choices as you lose pieces). Everyone plays chess in a serial spine manner. Not everyone chooses the same choices that the serial spine offers, throughout it's spine.
      \nThen as a matter of technicality, any game can fall under the definition of "serial spine", because any game can be described via the following serial structure:

      1. One player does something.
      2. Another player does something. Go back to step 1.

      Chess, at its core, is a lateral organization of serial modules, which is exactly what "lateral dislocate" games are, and what the majority of RPGs period are. In reality, any game has serial and lateral elements, so trying to pinpoint a game as one or the other based on a few choice details is ultimately fruitless. You have to look at the overall structure.

      Chess is not really a procedural game, because it's driven by a series of "state checks". Let's restate the game in lateral form:

      There are two colors: Black and White. White moves first. The players alternate moving one piece at a time.
      There is a specific setup of pieces.
      Each piece has a unique mode of movement.
      When a piece ends its movement on another piece, the latter piece is "captured" and removed from the board.
      When the King of either side is able to be attacked, this is "check", and that player must cause the King to no longer be in check.
      When the King is in check, and that player cannot take the King out of check, this is "checkmate", and that player loses.

      There's a few quirky rules (pawn promotion, en passant, and castling), but that up there is the base structure, a bunch of lateral instructions which nonetheless provide a pretty passable game.

      @2097,
      I definitely agree there. Serial hooks are necessary to understand a system which is largely lateral.

      \n Well, as a secondary thought, I think that, instead of classing a game as "serial" or "lateral", it's far more beneficial to discuss the presence and uses of serial and lateral structures in the same game.

      To that end, I'd regard lateral structures as helpful for laying out a deep system, where serial structures are helpful for directing and focusing gameplay to a particular end. Why? Because lateral structures (modules) interact in a large number of ways. Lateral structures are ultimately about defining various entities that will interact with one another in various ways. This allows you to utilize an emergent "rules physics", and to adjucate/predict reliably what happens when the game strays from the expected experience.

      On the flip side, serial structures focus gameplay on a particular experience (or set of experiences). However, each choice that a serial structure offers multiplies the number of possible outcomes that must all be accounted for. This naturally leads to a serial structure leaving outcomes with no means to adjucate them beyond common sense/fiat.

      One gives no direction; the other gives no way to judge the unexpected. The best answer is to hybridize them to great effect, and this is what most games do. Could they stand for more direction? Certainly. We generally call such explanation a "sample adventure" or "adventure module", though.

      \n I don't see how a game is inherently procedural or not. The split we're talking about is a matter of presentation, not of substance.       A relevant bit of research is that apparently people can only process about 7 new objects at a time. So presenting a procedure with more steps than that, although players could just follow by reading each step, a la the GURPs example upthread, will be very hard to learn.
