      \n

      You calling it lateral dislocate. But in lateral dislocate the game does NOT tell you to check anything.

      Tell me in chess, is it okay to skip checking whether checkmate has occured? Not really - because you've been told to do it by the game.

      In a traditional RPG, are you told you HAVE TO run the combat rules at any particular point?

      As I said, this is gunna be the big hurdle to get over, eg the hurdle being the notion that the state checks in chess and the various rule clusters in a traditional RPG are the same deal.
      \nZeroing in on this, because in the rules of chess, you are never explicitly told to check anything. Instead, the game outlines the objective of each player (module #1--also, having an objective does not make a game Serial Spine, else you could make a claim for any conceivable game being Serial Spine), a general turn order (module #2), and then the various means by which pieces move. The conclusions are left to be drawn by the players; that's what happens during the process of learning to play chess well. You are never told to choose between the Sicilian Defense or the King's Gambit to start off. You make a move, and the rules of the game tell you the consequences of that move. There is no "spine" connecting one move to another, save the spine which is crafted by the players. We call this spine the "strategy" of the player (or "tactics", in its short-term application). The existence of a player-created spine is essential to the definition of Lateral Dislocate, as you've set it out. (Either that, or what you call "Lateral Dislocate" is so narrow as to be utterly meaningless.)

      Also, I believe one could make the case that in a traditional RPG, you are implicitly told to run the combat rules in certain situations. Sure, the rules aren't technically 100% binding, but if a Serial Spine game must have 100% binding rules, then you cannot adjucate it to any degree.

      So, then. I actually believe that one could make an argument that traditional RPGs qualify for your serial/procedural designation.

      1. A Choose Your Own Adventure book would be an example of tightly-restricted Serial Spine, right?
      2. However, you've also allowed for GM adjucation to interrupt the strict flow of the Serial.
      3. So, "a CYOA book where you have the freedom to do things and have it adjucated" would be an apt description of SS.
      4. That's one of the cliche definitions of a traditional RPG. (I'd challenge anyone to find a meaningful divergence between #3 and traditional RPGs.)

      Further--I believe that there's a confusion being made between how a game is presented and what a game implicitly is. You could present a game in a serial or a lateral style, but that doesn't make the game inherently serial or lateral. I suggest stepping back from the notion that a game must be classed as one or the other, and noting that pretty much all games qualify as both, to a degree. Emphasizing one element over the other is a potent tool for communicating certain portions of the game in different ways, and understanding the benefits and drawbacks of each is crucial to game design.

      (I'd also recommend you check out more games. The ones I mentioned with a strong Serial Spine presentation are pretty prominent for the way that they mess with typical RPG expectations.)

      Zeroing in on this, because in the rules of chess, you are never explicitly told to check anything.       \nAnd how checkmate is achieved. Just focusing on checkmate for now, if you think it doesn't tell you to explicitly check for checkmate, how would you write it in the rules of chess to make it explicit you are to check this?\n
      You are never told to choose between the Sicilian Defense or the King's Gambit to start off. You make a move, and the rules of the game tell you the consequences of that move. There is no "spine" connecting one move to another, save the spine which is crafted by the players. We call this spine the "strategy" of the player (or "tactics", in its short-term application). The existence of a player-created spine is essential to the definition of Lateral Dislocate, as you've set it out. (Either that, or what you call "Lateral Dislocate" is so narrow as to be utterly meaningless.)
      \nIt's not essential to LD - it's not even related to LD - I would know.

      I've described this\n
      A serial sequence can offer you 100 choices. Sequentially you can go onto one of the choices which, lets say, offers another 100 choices. Sequentially going onto one of these offers a further 100 choices.
      \nI'll render it down even further

      1. Choose between eating a pizza or a burger.
      2. Go to step 1, but on your third arrival at step 2, end this procedure.

      Yes I know you think if you choose "pizza, pizza, burger", that you're choices (P,P,B) are a 'player crafted serial spine' and you think the choice between pizza or burger is essential to the defintion of lateral dislocate.

      No, you are all wrong and turned inside out!

      If you want to find out how I think that's the case, you'd need to empty your cup for a moment. Or if you think you know what the terms here mean (ones I've put together), okay, but I'd just leave it there saying no, you really don't.

      2097 seems to get what I'm talking about, so it's not unengageable.\n
      1. A Choose Your Own Adventure book would be an example of tightly-restricted Serial Spine, right?
      2. However, you've also allowed for GM adjucation to interrupt the strict flow of the Serial.
      3. So, "a CYOA book where you have the freedom to do things and have it adjucated" would be an apt description of SS.
      \nNo, it'd be an abomination of the term.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\n2097 \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nMarch 2013 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      There are two colors: Black and White. White moves first. The players alternate moving one piece at a time.
      There is a specific setup of pieces.
      Each piece has a unique mode of movement.
      When a piece ends its movement on another piece, the latter piece is "captured" and removed from the board.
      When the King of either side is able to be attacked, this is "check", and that player must cause the King to no longer be in check.
      When the King is in check, and that player cannot take the King out of check, this is "checkmate", and that player loses.
      \nWhat makes this read \u201clateral dislocate\u201d to me is that instead of step by step instructions, there is a bunch of weird facts related to the game. If you were to garble the order they are presented in you would get closer to the typical RPG text.

      I tried outlining a step-by-step description of chess earlier in the thread.\n
      So you've got all these RP gamers around talking about 'the way', and maybe you've never really seen that boardgame culture is a vibrant and energised event. And so with no alternative, you've never considered pitching 'the way' in the bin for the alternative (because there seemed to be no alternative) and instead simply taking the vibrant boardgame culture on, with serially written games which are part boardgame, and part allow someone to choose amongst options the rules present them, based on their reaction to fiction other people speak.
      \nI\u2019ve played boardgames all my life, though. Eurogames, American Thematic, abstract games, traditional card games\u2026
      I\u2019ve had some pretty bad experiences with them, too\u2014at least as boring as your Warhammer thread. Someone who is arguing and contesting every single move of the game. I was in a horrible game of Mice and Mystics the other day.
      \u201cI want to go here, to fight these bugs!\u201d
      \u201cNo, you don\u2019t, you should go here-and-here instead while J does this-or-that and I do this-and-that.\u201d
      \u201cOK\u2026\u201d (I followed the \u201cadvice\u201d.)
      \u201cOh, no, wait, you don\u2019t want to do that either because then the cheese tokens will XYZZY. Best just make a search roll for now.\u201d
      No mood, no nothing.\n
      Slowly a number of indie RPGs, like 3:16, seem to be doing this, but the authors seem to be in the closet about it.
      \nI think 3:16 is a shining star with its attempts to solve this issue we both have, and I\u2019ve been having it in mind during this discussion.
      It seems very clear from reading it how you \u201cdo\u201d things. So it\u2019s great in this regard. (BTW, that goes for the finished version of 3:16\u2014the free version, made for the contest, isn\u2019t nearly as good.)
      One thing that I absolutely love about 3:16 is its division between \u201cthe fiction\u201d and \u201cthe mechanics\u201d and clear points where the two interact.

      I didn\u2019t want to bring it up, though, because it also looks to me that it has somewhat serial play (a sequence of planets, on each planet a sequence of attacks).
      I want to rephrase one of the D&D B/X clones into just as straight forward, but to offer more choice towards the players. (An area of hexes you can go to, and in some of them there are dungeons you can take multiple paths through.)
      But I want to codify a very turn-based structure that I see hints of in B/X.
      \u2014 What do you want to do?
      \u2014 We go to the hex north east from here.
      \u2014 It\u2019ll take (\u2026 looks over how heavy they\u2019re packed) eight hours to get to the center of it.
      \u2014 Fine.
      DM rolls some dice, finds out there is a lair of evil sphinxes.
      \u2014 After six hours of travel you see [start describing lair].
      \u2014 We try to sneak around it.
      etc etc

      And in the dungeon, turns are ten minutes unless in fights.
      \u2014 You see a hall with an altar made of what looks like deep-red glass. There are exits east and northwest.
      \u2014 We strike every square of the floor with our ten foot poles, from a distance.
      \u2014 It takes ten minutes. Nothing happens.
      \u2014 We go east.
      \u2014 It takes ten minutes [or maybe the GM just checks of a ten minute mark in her book]. You see [\u2026]

      According to the rules, it takes less than ten minutes to travel through already explored areas.
      I need to work all these kinks out\u2014you can see that I\u2019m not there yet.
      And once I got that worked out, I want to somehow make it work for urban fantasy (like Buffy).
      Not sure it\u2019s possible\u2026
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n
      \n

      1. Choose between eating a pizza or a burger.
      2. Go to step 1, but on your third arrival at step 2, end this procedure.
      \nQFT. Here you can end up with eight completely different stories. Expand it a bit and you can make it an infinite number.
      Callan, I\u2019d I want to change things up so that
      1. players can make marks on the world, change it.
      2. the end game is more flexible
      3. there is room to do unexpected things. you have a list of options + \u201cother\u201d.

      How about Burning Empires? It follows an interesting campaign structure. You have to skip ahead to the \u201chow to play\u201d chapter near the end to see it.

      Just as a weird example, but would this be anathema:
      1. Present the players with a pizza and a burger.
      1a. Players can take one of them.
      1b. If they want to take more than one they need to roll a five on a six sided die.
      1c. If they do anything else like take one half, or leave both, or poison them, let them.
      2. Go to step 1 until the group has had enough.

      \n I think what Apocalypse World tried to do was \u201cPresent a fictional situation to the players. Let them describe how they interact with it. If their description includes X, follow rule X, if it includes Y, follow rule Y, if it includes Z, follow rule Z.\u201d

      timonkey,
      Okay, here's the difference. Let's look at a couple rules from D&D3. The first rule is that the Diplomacy skill can be used to convince NPCs to do stuff. The second rule is that the GM may grant players bonuses or penalties for situations of advantage or disadvantage. If the GM decides whether or not I get to roll my Diplomacy skill, I have to get his permission to use the rules. When the GM decides whether or not I get a bonus, we are following the rule because the rule says that that's what he's supposed to do.
      \nWith diplomacy, do you feel your owed or promised use of those rules?

      I'd think getting to roll at all is in itself an advantage like getting a bonus is an advantage?

      Clarification note: If the text is a serial spine one and the next step says you roll diplomacy, then if the GM/anyone doesn't, they are breaking the agreed upon activity. I'd just agree with you in this case, if you were refering to a SS game. In lateral dislocate, there's no agreement on what cluster of rules (like the skill cluster: diplomacy) is used next.



      Owed or promised? Those seem pretty emotionally charged words, but yes. I'd say I expect to use those rules, because they are the rules of the game! I don't understand why the fact that the game text isn't hyper proceduralized means that the GM has authority to ignore the rules any moment he feels like it with no reason or explanation.

      Please clarify: are serial spine vs lateral dislocate terms for the game text, or the game itself?

      \nYeah; it's sounding more like "serial spine" just means "explicitly spelled out" versus "lateral dislocate" meaning "common sense required".

      Owed or promised? Those seem pretty emotionally charged words, but yes. I'd say I expect to use those rules, because they are the rules of the game! I don't understand why the fact that the game text isn't hyper proceduralized means that the GM has authority to ignore the rules any moment he feels like it with no reason or explanation.
      \nI'd answer that question with because...common RPG gaming culture, that's why.

      Which is really a terrible answer.

      At its core, most traditional RPG play has very little to do with all of those various mechanics. All of that stuff, even the stuff we think of as core like character stats. hit points, combat rolls, whatever, that's all advanced, optional stuff that can be used or not used.

      Really, even the very stuff we'd consider essential.

      The real game is a simple verbal play back and forth. It's Mother May I with a fantasy theme for D&D. It's a Choose Your Own Adventure Book, built ad hoc with one player taking on the role of the author.

      As a game, cut down to its core, it's very simplistic. You could describe it in a page or two. And, it does involve a great deal of human judgment, mostly on the part of the GM, but also on the part of the character players when trying to entertain the GM with their responses and in doing so get him to respond with something they wanted to see happen next in the fiction being created.

      All that other stuff, it's all commonly used advanced techniques. Or maybe just tools, mostly for the DM to grab onto as they see fit, or because randomness sparkes creativity. Or because everyone agrees it's a bit more entertaining that way.

      Or at least that's what it all adds up to in very common practice.

      If you view it through that lens, there's a lot of stuff you can then do with it.

      You can make the whole thing vastly more procedural.

      Or, you could clarify what is going on in practice, make sure everyone is aware that's what is going on, and look at all of that other stuff as just the optional tools they are in reality for classic RPG play.

      Callan seems to have been drawn to one possibility, myself to the other.
      \n

      CarpeGuitarrem,\n
      I think Tim's right here--there's a blurring between how a game is presented and what it is, in your definition.

      I know your responding argument pretty much backwards "Yes, LD doesn't present them explicitly!? So what?       \n
      If so...what if there is no such configuration and can't just play someone elses game, but you instead have to invent your own game (which raises the question of how much of the other authors writing do you include, if any, in your new game?)?
      \nMost of the games I\u2019m interested in are available under OGL or CC.
      The exception is GURPS so I\u2019ll just skip it.\n
      It's like trying to ask a programmer whether they are talking about the text form of the program, or the program.
      \nInterestingly, in the Lojban language there are two different words for these two things. The text form of the program is samselpla, a predicate with three valency places, and the program as it\u2019s running is samru'e, a predicate with five places. They are two very different things and work very differently\u2014they actually don\u2019t share a single argument.

      My own take on it is that rules text are source code. Games written in the encyclopedic, declarative style of your OP LD example dysfunctional enough not to compile properly. If you DO get it to compile you\u2019ll end up with something like the OP SS game.

      That\u2019s why I think that the rules of chess can be stated in both an SS and an LD form. Chess is simple enough that the LD form compiles fine, if it\u2019s complete\u2014your OP LD game isn\u2019t complete, and most RPG texts aren\u2019t complete.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Most of the games I\u2019m interested in are available under OGL or CC.
      \nOkay, but I don't understand you noting stuff like the time it takes to do things in dungeon, etc, from before? Do you have a problem with it in some way, or are you sharing that you've got your work sleeves up, sweat on brow, hammering something together? If it's the latter, hey, cool stuff! \n
      My own take on it is that rules text are source code. Games written in the encyclopedic, declarative style of your OP LD example dysfunctional enough not to compile properly. If you DO get it to compile you\u2019ll end up with something like the OP SS game.
      \nAnd you can't get it to compile, ever. If you add some instructions on which components to use and in what order (ie, a spine), that be a game you've invented (granted it'll be from alot of components someone else made (to use a programming analogy, it's almost all libraries and very little user code written in))

      It's partly why timonkey and CG need to refer to 'the game text' and 'the game itself' - instead of acknowledging it being them inventing which rules components to use next, seperating the game text into the two ideas allows them to explain the discrepancy of how play differs from the text. Any difference from the game text "Ah, see, that's the game itself!", they'd say.\n
      That\u2019s why I think that the rules of chess can be stated in both an SS and an LD form. Chess is simple enough that the LD form compiles fine, if it\u2019s complete\u2014your OP LD game isn\u2019t complete, and most RPG texts aren\u2019t complete.
      \nChess can't be stated in LD form unless you subtract the sequential order of instructions from it/remove its spine (at which point it ceases to be chess (or atleast ceases to be vanilla chess)).

      LD, definitionally, is not complete - it lacks that sequential/serial spine. It has components and...which component do we use first, if any? No idea. Because there is nothing that tells you which ones to use next (or which ones to choose from).

      At best you can try and obsfucate the rules for chess until it's so confusing it appears to be LD or until you've garbled it's description enough its actually lost part of it's spine and actually becomes LD for the absence.

You are right, those are different things, especially when the game presented in the rulebook is not complete. It's a little weird because the game is this kind of intangible thing. A couple examples:

      Chess exists as a game, and that's distinct from the various different explanations of the rules. So if I buy one chess set it has a rules book, and you buy another and it has different rule book with different words in it, but they both describe the same game. The texts are different, but the rules are the same, so they have to be different.

      Or take the concept of slapping. I can explain it to you by saying "it's hitting someone with the palm of your hand", but those words aren't slapping.

      Does that make sense?

      \n Callan:

      I've rewritten it to be more complete, I think this is at least as complete as your first game in the OP. I'm not clear what you're getting at here comparing this to another rewrite by yourself.

      Also, I think you misunderstood my chess rules example, as you said their had rules for rooks. My point was that both are complete and correct explanations of chess, but that the explanations are still different.

      Rewritten Game 4 (rules of game 1, structure of game 2):

      Introduction:
      This is a game for two players. The first player follows the instructions in Chapters 1-5. The second player, called the GM, follows the instructions in Chapter 6.

      Chapter 1: Character Generation
      You have a fictional character represented by 10 HP.

      Chapter 2: Combat
      When your character fights a monster, rolls 1D6 for each. If your roll is equal to or higher than the monster's roll, you beat it and can continue. If the monster's roll is higher, you lose 1 HP. If you lose all 10 HP, you lose the game and the game ends. Keep rolling until either you or the monster is beaten. You may describe a fictional account of your attack on the monster.

      Chapter 3: Monsters
      Whenever you travel 1 km at a time a monster appears and fights you.

      Chapter 4: Movement
      After creating a character, and whenever you've got no monsters to fight, you travel. Roll 1D6 - this is how far you travel in kilometers. Record the amount after traveling it.

      Chapter 5: Adventures
      You have a travel distance of 20 kilometers you need to make to complete this game. If you make it to 20km, you win.

      Chapter 6: GM advice
      If you (the GM) feel moved to do so, you may grant the player +2 added upon his dice roll when fighting a monster. If the player still doesn't beat the monster, he continues to use the +2 bonus until the monster is defeated.

      CG,\n
      Uh, I think that a toolbox is chock-full of options for anyone who wants options. I mean, I thought that was an obvious fact.
      \nIf no ones agreed to their use (or lack of use), then no, a toolbox does not provide you any choices at all. Pick up an RPG and call out to people passing in the street that they need to make a save vs petrification. They will look at you strange and keep walking. Clearly no, the toolbox doesn't provide you any options. You need an agreement.

      I think I know what happens at this point in discussion - it's rather like komradebob's comment, in that you say 'but everyone knows that they agree to...' and then I say 'how do they know?' and you say 'because...common RPG gaming culture, that's why'

      I'll put forward the idea that most peoples idea of 'common' roleplay culture is utterly uncommon to anyone elses. In broad strokes it might match up, but in particulars they don't match at all - people don't have the same agreement on the use (or non use) of things in the toolbox. When one of these missmatches coes up in play, it's...not a good moment. Witness a brief example I ran across today - "Take it back!"

      The toolbox grants you no options. Only people do. And they can't when the text is ambigious or just plain doesn't describe what they need to agree to.

      Or such is one of the ideas of the thread. If you don't agree, okay - I've presented the idea, anyway, so mission accomplished in regard to that.

      Callan can you name a non-hypothetical game that does not tell the player when to roll a die?
      \ntimonkeys game 4. Just focusing on skill rolls for now (though it occurs with plenty of other rolls) D&D 3.X up to 5E (so far), underground RPG, cyberpunk RPG, warhammer (old edition, dunno about the new - mostly likely the new edition as well), blue planet, riddle of steel, rifts...I could go on.

      \n I wanted to wash my hands of this thread if Callan\u2019s case is that all of RPG:ing ever is a futile endeavour that has never occured. I simply do not agree with that.
      But I want to back up the lesser claim that many modern games\u2019 rules texts are a garbled LD mess with missing pieces.
      AD&D 2. How do you get experience points since they removed XP for gold? It has a bunch of \u201cfictional triggers\u201d but unlike going out seeking treasure, some of it is things that are pretty hard to achieve as a player.
      GURPS. It has reaction rolls that I guess you roll when you meet people (GURPS Social Engineering has more situations). But how do you meet people, when and where, and who creates these people, their predisposition and stats?
      This is where I find OD&D for all it\u2019s gaps to be remarkable. You find these-and-those monsters on this-or-that dungeon level. In the wilderness you roll on this-or-that table.
      Fiasco. What does \u201csuccess\u201d and \u201cfailure\u201d mean in even most scenes? You need to luck out with some strong needs and dangerous objects that players really PVP about in order to get some meaningful use of those two results.
      Three Sixteen. Hatred for Home. Presumably means you go and war against Earth. But does that mean that you can choose which planet you go to? Could you choose earlier among the planets?
      What happens if different characters get the HfH check box at different times? Do they just keep it boiling under, or what?
      Castle Ravenloft, the board game. You get a random treasure card before the session starts. What happens if that is a fortune or blessing? \u201cYou regain one lost hit point immediately\u201d. Gee, thanks.
      Fate. Many unclarities about how aspect use interacts with fictional statements. You perform a dance, also succeeding in the game mechanics, in order to put the aspect \u201cThe Crowd Favors You\u201d. The GM in the next breath, before you have a chance to use it, makes something happen in the fiction that causes that aspect to be invalid, fictionally. Do you lose it or not?
      Trail of Cthulhu. Spends. What if someone spends a point to search for a clue in an area where there are no clues? \xbbI go out in the forest and there I spend my botanical points\xab [sounds kind of gross]. You\u2019ve only prepped clues in the library, at the fire station, at the zoo and in the hospital, but not forest.
      Drakar & Demoner \u201991. The first game I ever played. (A European \u201cMagic World\u201d ripoff with some Runequest bits retrofitted and many original things bolted on) How do you even DO things. In retrospect, you should say things like \u201cI try to jump the chasm\u201d, you\u2019d take your skillvalue in chasm-jumping (or whatever, the closest one) and there\u2018s a table for CL which stands for \u201cchance of success\u201d which you roll on. But that isn\u2019t explicitly in the text for this \u201cbeginner\u2019s RPG\u201d. It\u2019s only implicit. And it\u2019s buried pretty far in one of the latter books in the box.

      It may well be that I\u2019m just terrible at reading game rules and that all of these examples are invalid.
      In which case I\u2019d need to find some new examples. But the counter-argument would be that all RPG texts ever are completely bug free and complete and very easy to understand and learn.

      Tulpa,

      The fun thing is you don't read the quote as allowing your character to use the skill of, say, diplomacy, when the spoken fiction has revolved around climbing a cliff. For you the text seems contrained in what kind of roll can be made. "You just wouldn't use diplomacy to climb a cliff!!" you'd say.

      I know I seem to create the strawman here - but then you refer to how the games are written. Write some programming code based around that quote and the program wont care if you use diplomacy or insight to climb a cliff. It really wont.

      You've ignored what is written and instead treat your subjective reaction to the text as if it's part of the text - you treat the 'can't use diplomacy to climb a cliff' notion as if it's in the text, when it isn't - the strawman is yours. Yes, remove the corrections - remove your strawman you're adding to the text. Then see how little the text actually tells you.

      But I know, it'll seem like I'm adding this ludicrous capacity to climb cliffs with sense motive or diplomacy - ie a strawman, and you'll correct me on how the game is written.

      It's weird though - I could write a tiny, silly little game where you use diplomacy to climb a cliff.
      1. You have a diplomacy skill - this is your only skill. It gives you +2 to a roll.
      2. You are facing a cliff. Use your one and only skill to climb the cliff, by rolling 1D6 and add the bonus. If you get a 4 or higher, you made it (go to step 3). Otherwise you plunge to your death.
      3. You made it! You win, congrats!

      If you try and insist you use climb, then you are just reading badly - inventing a strawman.

      Surely the only way my game works out is if you follow whats actually in the game text. Rather than adding restrictions which just aren't there in the text.

      \n Callan, here, make your point:

      Introduction:
      This is a game for two players. The first player follows the instructions in Chapters 1-5. The second player, called the GM, follows the instructions in Chapter 6.

      Chapter 1: Character Generation
      You have a fictional character represented by 10 HP.

      Chapter 2: Combat
      When your character fights a monster, rolls 1D6 for each. If your roll is equal to or higher than the monster's roll, you beat it and can continue. If the monster's roll is higher, you lose 1 HP. If you lose all 10 HP, you lose the game and the game ends. Keep rolling until either you or the monster is beaten. You may describe a fictional account of your attack on the monster.

      Chapter 3: Monsters
      Whenever you travel 1 km at a time a monster appears and fights you.

      Chapter 4: Adventures
      You have a travel distance of 20 kilometers you need to make to complete this game. You travel 1d6 kilometers at a time. If you make it to 20km, you win.

      Chapter 5: GM advice
      If you (the GM) feel moved to do so, you may grant the player +2 added upon his dice roll when fighting a monster. If the player still doesn't beat the monster, he continues to use the +2 bonus until the monster is defeated.

      \n Sorry, I had to run quick back there. I shall elaborate now. Callan seems to be operating around the premise that it requires a substantial logical leap to piece together traditional games like D&D. Now, maybe explanation quality has degraded some since then (I honestly don't remember), but I'm paging through the Moldvay Basic D&D PDF...

      Good night. It's all laid out, with crystal precision. "Here's what a GM is, here's the role players have, here's what a 'dungeon' is, here's what an 'adventure' is, and here's the basic actions you're taking." Plus a brief outline and summary of what's in each chapter, then a direct instruction to go to Chapter 2 to make your character.

      That's the opening chapter: oodles of very clear direction. I haven't finished reading yet, but so far, it belies that paradigm. That's why I say context is lacking. I'd also venture a guess that, really, if one honestly looked at the actual game texts (instead of relying on their memories of the impressions that the texts left), they'd find a lot more explanation than they remember.

      After all, we don't remember what we don't tend to use. And seasoned gamers don't tend to use that explanation.

      Could it be that a lot of the inclarities in many game texts stem from game designers taking D&D conventions for granted? I often find myself wishing that I had started with Moldvay instead of with the weird tertiary games that I first read.
      \nThat could be one reason, but it's also a tradition of not telling how to play the game. Callan's SS example in the first post tells the structure of how a session is going to be played (a sequence of play). That's what I'm interested in. Normally, the chapter for writing adventures are at the end of roleplaying books when it should be at the beginning.

      I skimmed through Hollowpoint this morning and it had the same structure as timonkey's example, which I feel is backwards. I had to puzzle things together in my mind to understand how the game worked. I think it would be more intuitive if timonkey's example instead had the structure 4, 3, 2, 1 (not necessary) and finally 5.\n
      Chapter 4: Adventures
      You have a travel distance of 20 kilometers you need to make to complete this game. You travel 1d6 kilometers at a time. If you make it to 20km, you win.

      Chapter 3: Monsters
      Whenever you travel 1 km at a time a monster appears and fights you.

      Chapter 2: Combat
      When your character fights a monster, rolls 1D6 for each. If your roll is equal to or higher than the monster's roll, you beat it and can continue. If the monster's roll is higher, you lose 1 HP. If you lose all 10 HP, you lose the game and the game ends. Keep rolling until either you or the monster is beaten. You may describe a fictional account of your attack on the monster.

      Chapter 1: Character Generation
      You have a fictional character represented by 10 HP.

      Chapter 5: GM advice
      If you (the GM) feel moved to do so, you may grant the player +2 added upon his dice roll when fighting a monster. If the player still doesn't beat the monster, he continues to use the +2 bonus until the monster is defeated.
      \nI remember one time when I let my (read: forced my) non-gamer, not interested girlfriend to create a character in my roleplaying game. She didn't understand what the skills were for, and I couldn't really explain it without showing it in a situation. So creating a character before you start playing seems really backwards in roleplaying games. You could create character elements that creates the adventures, but I don't want to read about skills or combat before I even know what kind of scenarios that I'm supposed to create and, more importantly, HOW to play them.

      It's like you say: people take a lot of conventions for granted.

      \n Hm? Any skill can be summarized as "When you want to do X, use the skill. Having a higher skill means that you're better at that." Well-named skills are self-explanatory.

      I think the mention of Hollowpoint brings up the reason why I'm bothered by the direction this discussion took. When viewed in the lens of rules-organization, yeah, it's a lateral design. But that's just a question of "how well are your rules arranged to explain the game?" I think that question is a red herring. The same game, as has been demonstrated, can be arranged in many different ways and yet remain essentially the same.

      The more interesting question, to me, is whether a game's actual form is serial or modular, when you take the appearance of the rules off the table. There, Hollowpoint is most definitely a serial game (even if the rules are organized in a modular fashion), because it's about the characters following a specific course of action and making choices along the way. If the characters wanted to open a barista, continuing the game would require everyone to reframe that barista in terms of the Objective-Conflict spine of the game.

      \n The so called \u201cModularity\u201d in that RPG design patterns book is great for computer programs\u2014DRY rocks, to keep a single point of truth\u2014and when developing the game so your rules don\u2019t contradict each other, but it makes it harder to learn, at least for me.

      Again, GURPS has rules that different types of being take wounds differently, and different type of damage works differently.       Just like the Zombie example that\u2019s praised in RPG design patterns.

      Don\u2019t get me wrong\u2014that\u2019s an amazing collection of patterns! I only disagree with its evaluation of those patterns, not the extraction or classification. I think some of the patterns he praises are very problematic.

      Step 0. How to play. Read chapter 1 and write down the 10 HP. Read chapter 5, write down the distance needed to complete an adventure. Read chapter 4 and roll that. Record the distance.       Hm? Any skill can be summarized as "When you want to do X, use the skill. Having a higher skill means that you're better at that." Well-named skills are self-explanatory.
      \nTalking about conventions. Here you assume how to use the skill. Why do you want to do X in the first place? How should you be able to know how the situations are created where you're about to use a skill? Look at how boardgames explain rules. They start with the win condition, then set-up and followed by a linear structure (a structure of play) for how to play the game. Even if you can do several things during one phase of the game (like doing AW moves), it's still in a structure of play. I talked about that earlier in the thread.

      Look at timonkey's example. Chapter one. I don't know what to do. Chapter two. I still don't know what to do. It seems like fighting monsters is a part of the game..?

      Look at my remade example. It "starts" instead with chapter four. Instantly you know what the game is about, and every chapter from there builds upon that. The same goes with how you play the game, or it becomes dislocated.\n
      There, Hollowpoint is most definitely a serial game (even if the rules are organized in a modular fashion),
      \nThat's what I think is so weird with Hollowpoint. They describe length and value in a dice roll - and have even an example - but it's not until some headlines later they describe what those terms really does. It's not until the following chapter that they actually explain why you do a dice roll. Up until chapter five, they say it's about "conflicts" ... whatever that means. It wasn't until they start talking about missions and why you have conflicts in mission that I understood why I should roll dice. They also talk about principals in chapter 4, but it's not until chapter 5 that they explain why you have principals and how to create them. It's backwards. It's all backwards.

      Now, what I meant was that (picking skills during) character generation have the same problem as length, value and principals in Hollowpoint. You can't really understand them until you know how the game should be played out.

      (Note: I think Hollowpoint have some cool ideas, but the game is a great example when they actually have a truly serial structure, but still uses the traditional structure of presenting the game, which is ... weird.)

      If anyone's real-life game experiences are anything like arguing on the internet, they should just give up gaming entirely. Immediately.

      Because it's one thing to bicker for a good long time on the internet, where it's all asynchronous and people are participating as much out of boredom and the need to do something with a few spare minutes as anything else, but if they're spending actual honest-to-god face-to-face time with a friend (or even a stranger) having a pointless circular argument driven by pure obstinacy and obstreperousness, then no one should ever sit down to play a game with them.       \nIt depends on whether it's
      A: obstinacy and obstreperousness
      or
      B: You just believe something and when someone else who believes just as much (but in something else) clashes with it, you call it obstinacy and obstreperousness.

      When a game relies on two people believing the same thing (what single skill is rolled when so and so fictional situation has just been described), but people being people they actually believe two different things - well, you can either go for an echo chamber of only gaming with people who believe the same things as you (change the people) or you can rework the system (change the rules). Granted, I'm talking pretty exclusively about changing the rules.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      \n\nAccountingForTaste \n\n\n

      \n\nMarch 2013 \n edited March 2013
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      When a game relies on two people believing the same thing (what single skill is rolled when so and so fictional situation has just been described), but people being people they actually believe two different things - well, you can either go for an echo chamber of only gaming with people who believe the same things as you (change the people) or you can rework the system (change the rules). Granted, I'm talking pretty exclusively about changing the rules.
      \nWhich is fine -- I think pretty much everyone is in favor of well-written rules, right? -- but if two people believe two different things to the point where they cannot find any kind of compromise at the gaming table and will instead argue about it forever, that's a problem that's at least 66% to do with the people and there won't be much even the best rulebook can do about that. And actually, a poorly-written rule is more uniting than anything else, in my experience: at least then they'll agree that the rule is poorly-written.

      (I don't think the "echo chamber" criticism even applies to a game. It's a fun activity you do with friends, not a legislature or news agency or debating society. People should play games with people they can get along with!)

      \n The reason why I think the skills are so self-evident is that they generally have little overlap. Athletics is for athleticy things. Diplomacy is for diplomacy-y things. Thievery is for Thievery-y things. Those skills also (at least in 4th Edition, as I recall) have pretty good examples of things that you're able to do. So you have skills, each of which let you do certain things.

      You're in a situation, and there's a problem to be overcome.

      What generally happens next is that players will then say "Can I use Skill X to do Y?" I think that most people, without having that explained, can pick up on that pretty simply. I mean, when you're in a situation, and you have things like "Athletics 5", "Religion 10", and "Survival 6" on your sheet, you're going to at least say, "Hey, what's this 'Survival' thing? Can I use it to do this?"

      Even better is when the book actually says what Athletics, Religion, and Survival can be used to do. Without having played D&D, I read through the 4th Edition playbook, and very quickly latched onto the how-to of play. And if I didn't have a clear idea, I knew that I could figure it out in play.

      I think the key of game design (and I'm summing up my points here instead of directly responding) is to give enough soft rails to give players something to go on. You can go tighter, but that's just a style choice.

      Ultimately, the difference between what's being claimed as "serial spine" versus "lateral dislocate" is this: a serial spine game is one where the game does a significant amount of hand-holding.
      \n

      Accountingfortaste,\n
      but if two people believe two different things to the point where they cannot find any kind of compromise at the gaming table and will instead argue about it forever, that's a problem that's at least 66% to do with the people and there won't be much even the best rulebook can do about that.
      \nThere's a ton a rulebook can do. There's just a major lack of assigned authority involved in traditional RPG's - it just leaves people to struggle with it. I'm guessing you don't see any difference to leaving two people to struggle with it Vs the text both parties agreed to assigning one of them the final say on the matter (the other one doesn't have to compromise, they just have to acknowledge the final say they agreed to)

      Anyway, you think a rulebook can't do anything. I'd disagree. Really either we discuss that a rulebook can handle this (before going on to anything else), or I guess there isn't anything to discuss otherwise.
      \nCallan, Callan, Callan, mon petite fromage, I took you at your word when your example was two people who cannot under any circumstances find a way to agree. It's a weird example, totally contrary to my own experience, but that may be because I won't willingly spend time with that sort of person anywhere besides the internet (and even then, only for the five or so minutes it takes to write a reply to their post, whereupon I blissfully forget their existence for another day or so).

      That kind of person from your example is going to argue with or without a book. If they agree that someone else has the final say on the matter, then they have already compromised. Won't the person from your example just disagree with the rulebook and/or use the rulebook as a stick to beat the other person with, since they firmly believe the one thing and will never, ever stop arguing their point? It's asking an awful lot of a rulebook to expect it to fix that person's brain.

      So, no, you're getting what I said wrong: I am not saying that a rulebook can't do anything. I'm saying it can't do everything. A good set of rules can minimize struggles, but some people (and please don't think that I'm necessarily pointing the finger at you, here) just like to argue, and will bend over backwards to continue their argument because that's all they want.

      Which leads neatly to your next bit:\n
      (I don't think the "echo chamber" criticism even applies to a game. It's a fun activity you do with friends, not a legislature or news agency or debating society. People should play games with people they can get along with!)
      \nThis is such a dismal, seemingly gamer expectation. That if you can't agree on a bit of fiction, you're not getting along - the dude could drag your ass out of a house fire, but if he keeps insisting skill X be used instead of skill Y in a fictional situation, that must mean you don't get along with him!?

      Okay, how I'd put your quote is if someone imagines things differently from you, you'd say you don't get along with them. I'd disagree. I think, at best, it's being picky.
      \nWell, you put my quote wrong, then. Not much I can do about that except try to explain it in shorter words? Are there shorter words? Let's try!

      I have friends. I get along with my friends. I don't always agree with them, but when we disagree about something, we can argue about it for a little while and we soon reach an answer that we can both live with. We still get along. That's what working out those different opinions is! A disagreement during a game isn't a failure of the game or the group, if everyone stays cool and actually works out a solution to it. It's great, it actually brings everyone closer together.

      But imagine I'm with the person from your example, the person who is absolutely, 100% opposed to whatever I think and will never, ever compromise. Every argument goes on forever, and there are a LOT of arguments. THAT is a situation where you cannot get along with the person, and that is a person you shouldn't game with. It's a game, you should play it with someone who is willing to play it with you.

      Going all Fox News on this idea and saying it's an echo chamber is, frankly, dumb. Play with people you can disagree amiably with, and who you can work out your disagreements with: that's awesome, it's so much fun, oh my god you will not believe how much fun it can be.

      Don't play with people like the ones in your example, the ones who just want to argue all the time over points that apparently will never, ever be settled. I don't know where you're finding those people, but wherever you found them, don't play with them. Let them save you from a house fire. Go ahead and argue politics with them. Dance at their wedding. Paint a magnificent portrait of them if the spirit so moves you, be their bestest friend EVER if that's how things can be, but if you cannot under any circumstances settle an argument in a game like friends, then don't play that game with them. Just don't. You will be a better person for it.

      \n Skipped reading most of thread like a pro. Just wanted to say that Lateral Dislocate looks a lot like declarative programming, while Serial Spine looks a whole bunch like imperative programming. Both styles have people who pathologically despise them, and individual programming languages tend to emphasize one at the expense of the other. But successful programs tend to use a mixture ideas from both, even if their programmers are not aware of it.

      Given the number of folk that balk at the idea of using sense motive to climb a cliff, either that is clear in the text and people en masse ignore it, or it isn't clear in the text and the assignment of a final say position is absent.

      Skipped reading most of thread like a pro.       \n\nbiggles \n\n\n

      \n\nMarch 2013 \n

      \n
      \n Not being 'complete' is an abstraction that enables easy hacking and free-play, though lol. There is literally no point in making a roleplaying game if you're fully specifying it because full specification is effectively writing a computer program. So that can't be what he means. Even if 'computer program' is a highly appropriate understanding of 'full procedure'. That said, imagined space certainly exists in computer games, so if Callan does mean what you say, conta is still using hyperbole when he implies there might not be room for imagined space.

      In terms of authority over rules disputes, I play with a group that is more likely to dispute the rules than dispute the GM. Taking people like me into account (this game is shit, we should fix it), the authority of rules as written is just as shaky as the authority of the GM in deciding the unspecified and which is taken more seriously depends on the RP culture you participate in. In my view, rules should be an agreement between participants. Fully specifying by a papal authority does nothing to secure agreement. In either case, the agreement is likely to be discursive and fluid. To resolve your disagreements, you must resolve your disagreements. This has little to do with how specific rules are, or how they are written.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nCallan_S \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nMarch 2013 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n AccountingforTaste\n
      That kind of person from your example is going to argue with or without a book. If they agree that someone else has the final say on the matter, then they have already compromised.
      \nWell, exactly - you've just identified that the person from my example has already compromised (if there is a final say assigned). So why do you think the guy from my example will just argue forever?

      The difference is here: when the text says who has the final say, the guy has agreed to that in advance of play (or otherwise he'd have declined to play).

      When no final say is assigned and then some other person decides to self promote themselves to the final say position, which wasn't agreed to before play - it sucks. Why does that other guy get to self promote himself to a position without even asking if anyone else agrees to that?

      Someone who'll concede to an assigned final say (or else they wouldn't have played) but will argue when there isn't one are not the same as someone who'll just argue regardless. If you're treating them both as the same, I'd have to leave it at saying I disagree that's the case. If you're saying it's only a 'always argue' guy and never one who'd follow rules on the final say if they were actually, you know, in the text, then I'd have to leave it as disagreeing with that. \n
      But imagine I'm with the person from your example, the person who is absolutely, 100% opposed to whatever I think and will never, ever compromise.
      \nThat example comes from talking with folks here - the moment someone here compromises a little away from 'hey, its all fine already', then the example is of someone less than 100% opposed. CarpeGuitarrem already gave a slight mention that technically I'm correct that any skill could be used. So that's a start towards a compromise.

      Biggles,\n
      There is literally no point in making a roleplaying game if you're fully specifying it because full specification is effectively writing a computer program.
      \nWith my serial spine example in the first post, does it fully specify whether someone gets the +2? Or does it just fully specify who's in charge of determining that?
      \nBased on a true/false input from the GM, yes.\n

      In fact even computer games don't fully specify - who lines up the head shot in an FPS game? The game code? No, the code just fully specifies that the real life player is in charge of this.
      \nYou're conflating specification of the internal mechanics of the game world with specification of the input protocols to the game world. It is in fact the conventions of play and player-culture that decides who aims, by the way. The code does not care whether any aiming takes place at all. It merely projects a space conducive to it. There is an important sense in which software games do fully specify - based on any given valid input and state, there is a procedure for computing the next state that will be represented in the output media. Given a particular seed to the RNG, these transitions are deterministic in a fairly obvious way.

      It should be fairly obvious that computer games do not specify user actions, user expectations, user culture, or user behaviour. But they do specify how the computer system responds to inputs, if in a dysfunctional manner at times.\n
      \n
      Fully specifying by a papal authority does nothing to secure agreement. In either case, the agreement is likely to be discursive and fluid. To resolve your disagreements, you must resolve your disagreements. This has little to do with how specific rules are, or how they are written.
      \nI'm not sure if you're refering to fully specifying the final say - if you're saying even if the text assigns the final say that folk will ignore the call of the person with final say, then...that's just cheating.

      Above I've granted what I'm talking about does in part involve 'change the people' - so in regard to this I would go into Accountingfortaste's 'don't play with these people' territory.

      I'm just saying before I change the people, I try and make sure they aren't just arguing because the text doesn't assign final say (and someone at the table is trying to act like they've agreed to them having final say, when they know they haven't agreed to that). That can be cleared up (really easily). Cheating - no, just don't play with them or if one is inclined to, realise its dysfuctional play to continue it and continue.
      \nHaha. I don't think you understand. It's not that this ruins the game for anyone at all. The contract to disregard rules as written if they are agreed to be stupid or limiting underpins our play. Disputed changes deemed to be required for internal consistency come into effect in the next session. The status quo is that we immediately assign ourselves the ability to modify whatever we're playing however we feel like, GM usually having preference in terms of setting the tone, inventing the universe (moreso in single-GM universes). We don't cheat, but cheating is not defined by violating the rules as written, but rather by behaviour that ruins the game. Defects in the rules as written are patched when discovered, so there is little point in trying to cheat in this way anyhow. In short, the game is the master of its own destiny. I would really rather not play with people who don't do this, because it's what I'm used to, and because not being able to dispute rules that suck sounds lame. The rules can suggest authority, but ultimately, it's a hint for good practice.

      If serial spine is not writing in an imperative procedural manner, fully specifying the internal mechanics of the game, or formally specifying authority, then it's Very Mysterious Indeed. Perhaps apprehending the difference between the good and proper construction of a game that really is an RPG and the foggy quagmire of lateral disconnect is lost on me.

      While LD evades such scrutiny - an LD RPG doesn't require you to use any of their components, so, to draw upon a real account I heard once, you could say you'd played the robotech RPG but the RL two hours of play involved narrations of driving around in a jeep in the desert and that's it. LD ends up being like that awful phrase about being in love - LD means never having to say you were wrong.

      Then we get weak sauce measurements in gamer culture 'were the players happy?', when if you applied that notion to chess and someone was unhappy, even mildly, at losing, then that would somehow indicate a problem with the rules of chess.       Biggles,\n

      \nOften the programmer has no particular intent with regards to how & by whom an object will be controlled when we write the input handling code. Many times it's abstract so that a number of different sorts of things could control the game. Not that this fact is particularly relevant to the overall discussion. Additionally, (and this is slightly more relevant), often game programmers write our games with the intent that they will be modified for unknown purposes in the future by ourselves or others. We certainly don't have the power to specify in code who will aim. We only have the ability to try to produce the conventions of play, and to interact with the player-culture to shape their interpretation and use of the game. This can be through the software or otherwise. While in Counter Strike, most aiming is done by humans or aimbots, there is a totally legitimate (and kind of awesome) speedrunning culture around Ocarina of Time that doesn't follow the implied conventions of the game's plot at all. It's my view that it's an important humility for game developers to understand that your players create your games too, and not in a trivial way.

      The more central point I was making there is that there is in fact a way in which computer games fully specify - they fully specify the internal physics of the game space. This is only relevant if SS is about full specification of the game's internal mechanics, though.\n
      \n

      \n CG,
      So, I finally "get" what Callan is talking about. I went to the following site:
      http://www.d20srd.org/index.htm
      And looked at the skills in question. Now, reading the descriptions for Climb and Diplomacy, it makes it pretty clear that you cannot use Diplomacy to climb a well and/or cliff.
      However, I did notice two things:
      1) There is no text telling a GM when to call for a roll in the SRD. In fact, I couldn't find any GM advice (maybe the SRD assumes the DM has a DMG?).
      2) There are areas of ambiguity which would require interpretation. Look at the descriptions for Ride and Handle Animal. Here they "try" to differentiate these Skills, but I can easily see checks for one being applied to checks for the other.
      And there is no text to tell you who decides who is right or when it is really needed at all. And furthermore, there is not a good advice as to when/how often these skills should be called for. There is nothing to prevent the GM from creating an Adventure where the PCs cannot proceed until they make a DC30 Craft Skill check. this is obviously bad gaming, but it is supported by the rules and a DM would be within his rights to do just that. I honestly feel like the game text can't fix bad gamers. But in this case, the DM might not be a bad gamer, he just wouldn't know it was a bad idea until he tried it or came across that advice.

      Callan has a good point, unfortunately, he is relying on the reader's ability to infer a LOT of information from his posts. It wasn't until halfway through the second page of posts that I even knew what he was talking about.
      I think that the pervasive culture of assuming that the reader understands what a Skill is, what it represents in the setting/game world and when it should/shouldn't be rolled is an example of how dysfunctional gaming happens. And to be honest, I think many gamers have had that moment where the GM says "make a climb check" and your reply is "can I use Acrobatics?" In most groups, the GM will think about it and make a ruling. But, that should be in the rules and a ruling shouldn't be required, should it?

      Callan, correct me if I am wrong, but I think I get what you are saying now and I think it is a GREAT point to make.

      And there is no text to tell you who decides who is right or when it is really needed at all. And furthermore, there is not a good advice as to when/how often these skills should be called for. There is nothing to prevent the GM from creating an Adventure where the PCs cannot proceed until they make a DC30 Craft Skill check. this is obviously bad gaming, but it is supported by the rules and a DM would be within his rights to do just that.       \nExactly!

      In fact I'd say this the dreadful extent of this is far ranging, where we talk about game that's 'all about imagination', but when A: the system allows them to enact a DC30 craft check and B: The way their imagination indeed goes is to have such a high DC roll, we call it a bad idea. A bad way to imagine.

      We tell them it's about their imagination. Then we tell them their imagination is wrong - it's bad/'it's a bad idea'. But then we have game books which don't tell them what they can do/are LD and allow a DC30 craft check, and in doing so we give the message it's okay. Then come play, we say it's bad.

      It's an awful culture of cross messages. It's poisonous.

      All stamping on what, while they might be kooky imaginations, are perfectly valid imaginations! Oddball imaginations are a good thing! It's actually okay to imagine starting with a DC30 craft skill - but which gamer veterans here would imagine such a thing? You wouldn't because you've been trained to think it's bad gaming - and why have you been trained to think it's bad gaming? Because since the game text doesn't force you to use a DC range of, say for example, 10 to 18, instead your imagination has been battered into the shape of 10 to 18 by various negative feedbacks (or reading about various negative feedbacks (what you call 'advice')).\n
      I think that the pervasive culture of assuming that the reader understands what a Skill is, what it represents in the setting/game world and when it should/shouldn't be rolled is an example of how dysfunctional gaming happens. And to be honest, I think many gamers have had that moment where the GM says "make a climb check" and your reply is "can I use Acrobatics?" In most groups, the GM will think about it and make a ruling. But, that should be in the rules and a ruling shouldn't be required, should it?
      \nI don't think this example, in as much as being a mild one, quite covers the problem.

      The problem is people will act like it's a rule that you cannot use diplomacy to climb a cliff - they will then tell the other person it's a bad idea (particularly when there is no final say power given to the other person), giving negative feedback to the way the other person imagines things.

      But as much as you can have the DC30 craft check, there is no rule that restricts a call for a diplomacy roll to climb a cliff in a traditional text.

      It's not breaking the rules. All you have is one person thinking how they imagine it is the rules, and in doing so stomping on the other persons imagination. It's poisonous. It's the very antithesis of a game about imagination.

      I say protect imagination, by recognising where you've been left with absolutely no rules on a matter - the DC30 craft check is fine because of the lack of rules, the diplomacy to climb a cliff example is fine because of a lack of rules. The other person is not doing it wrong, they are not imagining it wrong, the rules just give sweet FA restriction on the matter.

      Anyway, a example of 'false associative' came to mind, so I thought I'd outline it\n
      1. Read step 2, then step 3 becomes the case.
      2. There are four types of Blast : Fire, Ice, Acid and Black hole.
      3. A fire elemental stands before you, wreathed in flames, it's every breath the excess of a furnace, it's eyes pouring plasma. It raises it's hand and from it's palm shoots a Blast at you!
      \nThe questions for anyone are - A: what blast do you think of?, and more importantly both B: Can that be the only type of blast that it'd do? And C: If someone else employs another type of blast than that, are they cheating?

      \n JD,
      I put a CD30 Craft Check forward as an example of a bad scenario design. That the adventure cannot continue until a PC passes a DC30 crafting check. I could have gone with the more obvious Spot check, but in a Pathfinder/D&D group, not many PCs are likely to have Crafting and depending on their levels, they probably might not have enough Crafting to pass a DC30 check.
      But, until the DM makes this adventure, and it fails horribly, this information may not be intuitive, obvious or even properly understood by the novice GM. Sure, this is fixable, but it is an example of why we need rules telling the GM what they can't do, when to call for a check, what kinds of checks are appropriate, etc. I am sure this is covered in the DMG to some extent, but I am equally sure that there is not text forbidding a DM from calling for an impossible skill check before the adventure can proceed. Don't get me wrong, these high DC numbers are appropriate to the setting, genre and fiction of the world and play at the table. But if play cannot proceed until an impossible roll is required, something has gone wrong. The designers of these games know that this is not a good idea, but that knowledge is sometimes not encoded in the rules. And many times bad play is not disallowed by the DM.
      I think its a problem, I think it is solvable and I think it is a good idea to address it in future designs.
      I don't know if I would use such a hyperbolic statement as to say it is poisonous, but I would go so far as to say that this sort of rules structure has ruined some games for me on more than one occasion. And from what I know of my fellow gamers, I think this is a scenario that other GMs and players have experienced. Whether or not it ruined their play experience will vary wildly, but I know that this is a real issue we can solve.
      Dave M

      \n I'm trying to think of a situation where "play could not proceed" based on a failed skill check of any kind in a D&D or Pathfinder game, because TPKs are completely fine in both those games, but if that situation would be bad for a particular group, which I can imagine, either we can adjust the game text to solve that problem for that particular group (proceduralize it), or encourage that group to adjust their game to their taste (push the 'play as creative act' interpretation of the traditional RPG), but I don't see one approach as being inherently better than the other. They just pursue different goals.

      or encourage that group to adjust their game to their taste (push the 'play as creative act' interpretation of the traditional RPG)
      \nI think alot of the problem is tied up in this notion. If we tease it apart a bit...
      - Why did the GM have a DC 30 check if that wasn't to his taste already? It's already to his taste.
      - If the game was written with the intention it's for the groups taste, why is the text supposedly saying the GM controls this (rather than say some kind of vote system - perhaps like universalis has?). If it's for the groups tastes, it's giving the wrong message. Poor wording.
      - And what happens if you push the play as creative act and...the GM (pretty much in line with the text about 'who's in charge') says no? Push harder?

      The group creative, in as much as it's in peril of simply being told 'no', really isn't supported by the text. That's pretty much why some groups avoid the text like the plague and declare proudly about how they didn't roll all night.

      I hesitate to mention that what's often treated as a supposed counter weight to being told no is that the group implies they will cease playing under that GM.

      Too much walking on egg shells, by my measure. Particularly too much to consider it as about equal to proceduralisation.

      \n I talked above about it being a problem to treat the person who will argue when the rules fail to assign authority as the same as someone who'll argue regardless of the state of the rules.

      \n That second paragraph makes zero sense.

      Yes, under some circumstances and in some times and places calling for a difficult Craft check is a mistake. It can be a mistake of many different kinds: not expressing clearly how you set up Craft checks, making a mistake when following that principle, being drunk or petty, or whatever. This does not mean the "GM sets the Craft DC number when not following the table" rules are broken, it means that human beings will play the game and may make mistakes and the designers (and the huge number of people who prefer their gaming this way) value giving the GM role great improvisational and expressive power over proceduralizing beep boop every little thing they might do to avoid people making mistakes.

      There is a massive human upside to designing rule sets like this.

      \n Wait, has this devolved into whether or not rules should explicitly legislate against impossible (for the given characters) checks that must be passed to advance the plot? D20's numberwang-based check system aside, isn't not having impassible walls common sense?

      Also, I am a big fan of puzzles, and "you cannot use Sense Motive to climb a cliff" sounded like a puzzle to me. So here's my solution. I present to you my work:


      Rightly And Properly Using Sense Motive To Climb A Cliff: The Definitive Guide By Biggles

      1. Brew a very strong, magically enhanced psychoactive potion over the course of a very long time. The effects of the potion are to place the user face to face with the universe itself, represented as a highly conversant four-year-old holding a samurai sword.
      2. Cripes, what goes on in this alarmingly dangerous conversation is somehow transformed into movements of the drinker's body. The place in the conversation corresponds to the drinker's situation. The cliff is a tantrum. Somehow, placating the child will enable you to navigate the cliff-face. If only you knew what it wanted.
      3. Sense motive. (N.b. I don't actually know what this skill is for b/c we don't have it in our games. I'm assuming that you can use it to detect the nature of a tornado-katana-tantrum.)
      4. Goddamn that's a high cliff, how did I get here? What's wrong with kids these days?

      I also thought of one where the cliff just had a face and you had to figure out whether it was trustworthy, but it wasn't as funny and didn't involve having Sense Motive boost your actual climbing skills. Obviously a less extreme version of the above is possible in which you merely become as-one with the body and your universe, and can use sense-motive to feel your way through life for five minutes. So there we have it. The SRD is clearly wrong. You can and should use Sense Motive to climb a cliff. Have a nice day.

      Also, I am a big fan of puzzles, and "you cannot use Sense Motive to climb a cliff" sounded like a puzzle to me. So here's my solution. I present to you my work:
      \nHuh. My solution was simpler:

      You gotta climb this cliff. It's tricky, though, unless you know the correct path upwards. Fortunately, there's a local guide at the top of the cliff who knows the way. Unfortunately, he totally wants to see you fall to your death.

      Roll Sense Motive to track his glee or disappointment as you consider which handholds to use on your way up. Keep choosing the ones that make him unhappiest, and you'll be up there and kicking him in the junk and taking his stuff in no time at all!


      (Although I suspect that in most groups I've played with, the most a successful Sense Motive roll in that situation could do would be to either give you a bonus on your Climb check, or reduce the difficulty on the Climb check. Surprisingly(?), gaming groups in real life rarely have arguments as weird as these hypothetical gaming groups do.)

      Biggles/AFT,
      My own idea was that the cliff was an earth elemental and you needed to be nice to it to be able to get up it (which I made up after thinking of sense motive/diplomacy to climb a cliff). All the imaginary ideas in your examples seem cool and valid. Once you accept the unconventional connections that are possible, it can be a source of creativity, exemplified by your examples. I'd describe the traditonal RPG's skill system as lateral dislocate, in that yeah, you could call for any skill (or none at all), but if unconventional connections are accepted rather than treated as wrecking the game, I think that works out. Try and treat it as if only certain skills can be used at certain times, and you get into trouble given the LD structure.\n
      isn't not having impassible walls common sense?
      \nDepends on how much you think imagination works by common sense? I think if imagination worked purely by common sense*, then there would be no magic imagined, no monsters imagined - infact, not even any fictional people or fictional objects imagined. It depends how much you bring common sense into imagination - and if someone else brings it in less, are they wrong to do so and you're right?

      When I wrote diary of a skull soldier I put in some RL time limits, to avoid any particular imaginary act just dragging things on - which is similar to stopping a GM who requires a DC 30 craft roll (or he'll do nothing) drags things on. To me it was common sense as well - so much so I could write common sense down as a rule. If common sense is so clear, why can't we write it down? If we can't, perhaps things aren't so clear until they are written down?

      * Watching the simulationist culture over the years in forums, it's interesting how they've gone from a 'common sense' motif to a 'genre emulation' motif. I think over the years they kinda figured they were undermining their own imaginations to fetish common sense.

      \n Well, if you were making Kafka's Before The Law into a game, you might want a bunch of impassible checks to lie between the player and their ultimate goal. Or them really doing anything. So there are times when it would be legit to to impassible wall, I guess.

      The common sense I'm talking about here isn't a kind that has anything to do with realism or simulationalism - it's about whether or not the players are gonna finish your scenario, encounter, dungeon, whatever. If what you want to happen (usin yo imaginations) is impeded by an impossible (for the PCs) check that you somehow bottlenecked the whole scenario around, isn't it just that you messed up your scenario design? One would expect a GM in that situation to fudge their way out and learn from their mistake. I would rather not rule out bad scenario building because I fear that I may rule out some excellent scenario building in the process. *

      This, I guess, shows two approaches. One gives the GM/players instructions on how to run a game. The other gives a GM/players tools to make a game with, and expects the GM and players to know how they want to run the game, and use the tools if and when appropriate. Are these two approaches SS and LD? Or is LD free interpretative play with skills and other mechanics as receptacles and aids for the fiction, while SS is more akin to a board game that produces fiction (to put it crudely)?

      *That said, the DCxx check system has always been totally illegible to me and I wouldn't be totally shocked if people accidentally put check targets in the wrong place.
