      Well, if you were making Kafka's Before The Law into a game, you might want a bunch of impassible checks to lie between the player and their ultimate goal. Or them really doing anything. So there are times when it would be legit to to impassible wall, I guess.
      \nI think this is the key thing - if you were making before the law into a game but you let the GM choose any DC, well he's probably going to bring his traditional notions of play and choose DC's between 10 to 18 - thus missing the cookyness of the scenario you're trying to make into a game. In this case you aught to make the minimum DC 25+ in order to ensure they actually play your game, rather than play the game the same way they play other games.\n
      The common sense I'm talking about here isn't a kind that has anything to do with realism or simulationalism - it's about whether or not the players are gonna finish your scenario, encounter, dungeon, whatever. If what you want to happen (usin yo imaginations) is impeded by an impossible (for the PCs) check that you somehow bottlenecked the whole scenario around, isn't it just that you messed up your scenario design? One would expect a GM in that situation to fudge their way out and learn from their mistake. I would rather not rule out bad scenario building because I fear that I may rule out some excellent scenario building in the process. *
      \nI think the question is, how much are the game designers actually the ones to design the scenario? Does dogs in the vineyard, 3:16, capes, spione and my life with master have a partial amount of scenario design actually done by the game designer and built into the game? I'd say yes.

      Or if you want to say scenario design is to be left entirely up to the user, okay - though I think this clashes with pretty much any game with any sort of thematic element, like dungeons and dragons or vampire - it suggests that there will be dungeons, or vampire - these are all scenario elements as determined by the game authors. So it's not really leaving it up to the user to design a scenario entirely. It's just leaving all the math up to the user to figure out. Thus 2097's predicament.

      But regardless, if you or anyone wants to say scenario design aught to be left entirely to the end user, well, I have my disagreements on the practicality of that, but okay, that'd be the position of folks.\n
      The other gives a GM/players tools to make a game with, and expects the GM and players to know how they want to run the game, and use the tools if and when appropriate. Are these two approaches SS and LD?
      \nYou could have an SS or LD implementation of what you're talking about. I think universalis is an attempt at an SS implementation of what you're talking about.\n
      Or is LD free interpretative play with skills and other mechanics as receptacles and aids for the fiction, while SS is more akin to a board game that produces fiction (to put it crudely)?
      \nIt depends if you'd call a guitar a machine that produces music, or a brush, pallette and canvas a machine that produces art. Imagine playing a boardgame in much the same way you'd say you play a guitar - and producing creations from each, from playing them.

      SS is like playing a guitar.

      LD is like building a musical instrument (generally a whole new one every session!) and attempting to play it even as you build it! Then demolishing what you built afterward/let it fall from memory without writing it down, because it's beep boop if you just, like, have a pre built guitar to play.

      \n\nbiggles \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nMarch 2013 \n edited March 2013
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n hwell I didn't manage glean much from your last 2 quote-response sections other than that it's probs not providing-tools vs providing-procedure. So I'm gonna focus on the other two blocks because I have something to say about them other than "whats the definition, i don't get it." Could be because I'm more of a synthesizer/tracker person than a guitar person, idk.\n

      I think this is the key thing - if you were making before the law into a game but you let the GM choose any DC, well he's probably going to bring his traditional notions of play and choose DC's between 10 to 18 - thus missing the cookyness of the scenario you're trying to make into a game. In this case you aught to make the minimum DC 25+ in order to ensure they actually play your game, rather than play the game the same way they play other games.
      \nThere could be a more general game text that looks at Kafkaesque RPG. In my view, it would be more elegant to have a resolution system that entirely precludes any checks that will inherently never make sense than banning them. But it depends on the specificity of the rules you're writing. This is relevant to the next bit too.\n

      I think the question is, how much are the game designers actually the ones to design the scenario? Does dogs in the vineyard, 3:16, capes, spione and my life with master have a partial amount of scenario design actually done by the game designer and built into the game? I'd say yes.

      Or if you want to say scenario design is to be left entirely up to the user, okay - though I think this clashes with pretty much any game with any sort of thematic element, like dungeons and dragons or vampire - it suggests that there will be dungeons, or vampire - these are all scenario elements as determined by the game authors. So it's not really leaving it up to the user to design a scenario entirely. It's just leaving all the math up to the user to figure out. Thus 2097's predicament.

      But regardless, if you or anyone wants to say scenario design aught to be left entirely to the end user, well, I have my disagreements on the practicality of that, but okay, that'd be the position of folks.
      \nI think that in many cases, the scenario design should be left to the user of the rules.

      Specifically, when the rules being written are very general, the rules are only doing scenario design in a very implicit manner. Say a document only contains a resolution system. If people use those rules, the part of their scenario design that's being done by the rules is that the resolution system used is the one in the rules. Not much, but it's something, and it will alter the feel of the game. It's my guess that we're roughly on the same page in that respect - we view abstract rules as contributing to scenario design.

      In some cases, the end user will be a GM, who works straight from the rules and improvs through anything unspecified. In other cases, the end user will be a designer (possibly also a GM), who takes the rules given and builds more game text and rules on top of them. (And then uses them as above or distributes them or both.) Different rules will specify more or less of the game activity. I don't have any particular feeling about rules that specify more or rules that specify less. It is my personal taste to specify as late (in the design process) as possible, however, because unnecessary constraints may bite me in the ass down the line. I also like to avoid binding components together until I absolutely have to because it makes it easier to lift them out and use them elsewhere, or see how the game would look with a part removed. I certainly don't think that everyone should think how I think, though. I think like a particular kind of software designer. People should do what works for them.

      If a ruleset has an extremely laborious and highly obfuscated system for encounters/conflicts/etc (hi dnd) then it's gonna be bad to leave encounter design up to GMs. It's probably also gonna be a bad idea to leave running encounters up to the GM. Maybe playing the characters should be left to those who understand the system. Computer programs, for example. More legible rules might actually help users create & design their own scenarios rather than present them with mind-numbing elementary math problems. In the world at large, scenario design is left to somebody. As I don't think that scenario design is particularly hard providing the designer has a good vocabulary of game construction techniques and knows what they want to do, I think it's fine to leave it up to whoever the end user might be. If I am the end user of my own rules, so be it. Why treat users of rules as if they are less capable of scenario design than makers of rules? If it's hard for other people to grasp design in your framework, isn't that problematic in itself?

      For me it's not so much a question of to what extent the game designers do the scenario design (they certainly contribute in varying degrees), but a question of to what extent the GMs and players are the real game designers. I tend to think that in games (including software ones), the micro and nano level design/construction is as important as the high-level conceptual design. Although bad conceptual tools or theory can easily throw a whole work, alone they are nothing, and bad nano/micro can ruin the whole thing too.

      So yeah. In my opinion, it's good to leave users of rules room to fail. Game designers enjoy the same freedom, after all. Not doing so is also fine.

      I think that in many cases, the scenario design should be left to the user of the rules.
      \nDoes that mean you think dogs in the vineyard, 3:16, capes, spione and my life with master do not have any amount of the scenario written by the game designer into the system?\n

      \nThat's like saying if someone sells cakes, it's treating buyers as if they are less capable of baking cakes.
      (the cake in this example refers to a partially made cake, most of the base is done, but icing, any filling, etc can be added by the end user)

      It's nice to eat a cake someone else (atleast partially) made instead of just ones you made entirely yourself. It doesn't make you less of a cook to do so. That's no lie.\n
      So yeah. In my opinion, it's good to leave users of rules room to fail.
      \nI think it makes sense as an idea because you are stating it explicitly here.

      If you write an RPG that says somewhere near the front (or at worst, somewhere in the text!) that the game designer has deliberately left room in the game for users to fail at just running the game, I'd agree that's a heads up about the product and I think in as much, that could work out. Those who dig that will take it up.

      But the less that's explicitly said in the particular game text, the less I think it works out and the more I just think it's a BS idea. It'd be like selling a car without certain parts (break cables, for example) while saying nothing, then saying afterward 'Ah, see, I left room in the structure of the car for you to fail!'. But as said, saying a car has parts missing and attemping to sell it, that obviously can work out (there are likely people who want that anyway). I feel I'm being pretty moderate in just saying give some advance notice and it's fine, but I'm guessing a big responce over it?

      \n\nbiggles \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nMarch 2013 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n

      Does that mean you think dogs in the vineyard, 3:16, capes, spione and my life with master do not have any amount of the scenario written by the game designer into the system?
      \nWell, I haven't played those, although I do know about them. Given that immediately after that quote, I made an argument (which I expected you might agree with) that pretty much every RPG rule implicitly does some scenario design, I probably do think that they do scenario design. Based on what I know about those games, they probably do more scenario design for the GM (and more explicitly contribute to scenario design, including tone hints) than something like FUDGE or GURPS or Locus.\n

      That's like saying if someone sells cakes, it's treating buyers as if they are less capable of baking cakes.
      (the cake in this example refers to a partially made cake, most of the base is done, but icing, any filling, etc can be added by the end user)

      It's nice to eat a cake someone else (atleast partially) made instead of just ones you made entirely yourself. It doesn't make you less of a cook to do so. That's no lie.
      \nThe thing about cakes is that baking them is a non-reversible operation. If you were to ask me if I preferred a cake that was sold with its recipe to a cake that's sold alone, I'd strongly prefer the cake with the recipe. Even better would be a recipe with a conceptual explanation of the recipe creation process. In terms of this analogy, though, I'm not arguing that nobody should sell any cake. I'm arguing that free distribution of partial recipes is a decent and useful practice. So what if my roux recipe doesn't tell you not to put it in baked beans? I don't think that this makes it a bad roux recipe. Although. Lesson from my flatting life that other people may not be stupid enough to need: don't put roux in baked beans.

      The cake thing is also a bit of a misleading analogy, though, because a cake can be eaten and appreciated with no particular assistance, whereas an RPG requires a group to interpret and run it. There is interpretation in cake-eating but not usually of the same kind.\n
      \n
      So yeah. In my opinion, it's good to leave users of rules room to fail.
      \nI think it makes sense as an idea because you are stating it explicitly here.

      If you write an RPG that says somewhere near the front (or at worst, somewhere in the text!) that the game designer has deliberately left room in the game for users to fail at just running the game, I'd agree that's a heads up about the product and I think in as much, that could work out. Those who dig that will take it up.

      But the less that's explicitly said in the particular game text, the less I think it works out and the more I just think it's a BS idea. It'd be like selling a car without certain parts (break cables, for example) while saying nothing, then saying afterward 'Ah, see, I left room in the structure of the car for you to fail!'. But as said, saying a car has parts missing and attemping to sell it, that obviously can work out (there are likely people who want that anyway). I feel I'm being pretty moderate in just saying give some advance notice and it's fine, but I'm guessing a big responce over it?
      \nProblematic analogy here. A car required parts to run and kills people or does nothing if it does not work correctly. An RPG can be played with no written rules, and will merely embarrass, confuse, or bore people if it works wrong. Unless it goes REALLY wrong. But that's not gonna happen just because some mechanical rule wasn't included. Also, cars do allow users room to fail, hence the rather popular activity of crashing cars into each other and getting very hurt. Not that I am a fan of this. But motorsport fans might not enjoy systems that don't put drivers in full control of the vehicle. Cars don't say "you can crash your car", because it is a well known fact that driving is a skill that people learn to be competent at. Roleplaying is not as popular as driving cars, but it has always been my understanding that GMing a game and playing in a game are skills that people learn to use and grow over time. Games are known to fail depending on the competency of the group at these various skills. If a game gives even more room to fail than is the norm then sure, it might be nice to give notice. Or if you're selling it for some reason. But unlike car manufacture, the production of roleplaying game rules is not always centred around their use as a saleable commodity. Like recipes or software, they can be exchanged amongst peers with no warranty except to the minimum extent required by applicable law, and no thought for customer experience. I don't think that carefully commodified rules are any better than less commodified ones. If your target audience doesn't see GMing and playing as skills that help make a successful game, then you should probably put a warning or explanation. Otherwise, no point, they know the deal already.
      \n

      \n\nDInDenver \n\n\n

      \n
      \n JD,\n
      I'm trying to think of a situation where "play could not proceed" based on a failed skill check of any kind in a D&D or Pathfinder game, because TPKs are completely fine in both those games, but if that situation would be bad for a particular group, which I can imagine, either we can adjust the game text to solve that problem for that particular group (proceduralize it), or encourage that group to adjust their game to their taste (push the 'play as creative act' interpretation of the traditional RPG), but I don't see one approach as being inherently better than the other. They just pursue different goals.

      Not to mention, if we're really going to say "these texts are damaging" when we're talking about the most popular texts in the hobby, shouldn't we see way more damage? Somewhere?
      \nOK,
      RE: I'm trying to think of a situation where "play could not proceed" based on a failed skill check
      But if we look at other posts on SG, you can see examples of just hat in CoC posts as well as other games. DC 30 Craft in a D&D game is an extreme example, but a DC30 Spot Check to spot the clue that allows you to go to the next scene is a more common issue. What happens when every member of the party fails? The rules might describe how to decide when to roll, they might describe what happens when you fail. But they rarely go to the point of denying the GM the ability to shut down their entire adventure by requiring success on a particular action before the adventure can continue.
      Of course, a GM will eventually learn to stop doing that. But if the Designers already know this is a bad idea, why not write a rule forbidding the GM from asking for this check. Not a rule that says "don't call for a Skill Check that can never succeed" (although that is not a bad rule necessarily), but more like "Do not create a scenario in which play cannot continue until an impossible task is completed" rule. It seems obvious, but I know I flubbed this up when first GMing and I imagine others did too.

      I don't think this is an issue with inheriting previous design sensibilities. I don't think this is a case of asking a designer to try to map all the possible combinations. This is just a symptom of the culture assuming everyone will houserule a game, do it correctly and work through/around these common problems based on their own experiences. And if you are an experienced GM, eventually, you develop just such a toolkit that you use to deal with common isues.

      RE: adjust their game to their taste
      Sure, for many games, they need to be adjusted to taste. How do you do that? Again, if the game is playtested, you can answer that question as a designer. Otherwise, you are leaving gamers to somehow discover how to do this in every game they learn. You can't encode all of the designer's knowledge into a game, but you can encode the parts that you tweak to get different kinds of fun, right? And if you know there are actions/rules mods that are just un-fun, why not just disallow those actions?

      RE: shouldn't we see way more damage?
      Again, I wouldn't go so far to say these texts are poison, etc. But, I would go so far as to say that I have had my own fun ruined and I know of other players who have had their fun ruined by a Rules As Written legal action performed by another player or GM that could have been prevented with a slight rules tweak. My sample is not huge, but does it have to be? I don't think this is an unreasonable request. Write rules preventing the GM, or any player for that matter, from doing something that ruins the fun. If you keep it in terms of the Rules As Written, you don't have to worry about creating rules to manage the players at the table, just the characters and their fictional actions. If you look at D&D or Pathfinder, they have rules for saving movement points from turn to turn, standing on the sides/intersections of squares and hexes. Why not the same rules for skill checks, declaring actions/reactions, etc? When is it legal to use Diplomacy to scale a cliff? When is it illegal? Players can come up with justifications for anything in a fictional universe, what is the limit?

      I get what you are saying JD. It is impossible for a game designer to encode the entirety of their knowledge of a game into a given rulebook. And even harder than that to regulate play outside of the shared adventure at the table. But I do not think it is unreasonable to expect that bad game play can be pointed out or even prohibited in the rules. Do you?
      Dave M
      \n

      \n Hi Callan,

      I think your Serial Spine example is a nice illustration of how a moment of communicating fiction (I fire a flaming arrow; do I get a +2?) can be inserted into a functional dice game (roll until I win or lose).

      I'm trying to find a way to actually make this an RPG (of the sort I like) by pumping up the relevance of the fiction-communication part, all without destroying the completeness of the dice game. I suspect this is where the actual challenge lies.

      Here's my first stab at it:

      Your character has three possible destinations, each at a different distance, each addressing a different need, each offering a different reward. (A) has your sick mother and a fearsome trumpet and is 17 km away, (B) has your kingdom's battle line and a sacred chalice and is 14 km away, (C) has your temple and a healing potion and is 20 km away.

      For every 6 miles you travel, roll 1d6 + (total number of miles / 6). On a 6: without the potion, your mom dies; without the trumpet, your kingdom is breached; without the chalice, your god abandons you.

      Each time you reach a destination (A, B, or C), your hit points return to full.

      Choose which destinations to seek in which order.

      If you have not died, the game ends when you reach your third destination.

      Otherwise, play as Callan described.

      Now we have a game where a player can invest in the fiction because their decisions affect it.

      In order to earn +2 from the GM, narrate something that they enjoy hearing.

      Now we have a game where fiction can be narrated with purpose.

      If I'm a good entertainer, that helps my odds at the dice game, which helps my odds of getting the fictional results I wanted.

      The GM still doesn't do anything with the fiction, though, except give it a thumbs up or a thumbs down. No RPG fun for them. We need to fix that. If we can fix it so that the GM and I are being creative in tandem, rather than separately, that would be better still. In fact, that may be an absolute requirement.

      The final thing I might require is "reading the fiction".

      Oh, and there's also the issue of having some non-mechanical basis for prioritizing mom / kingdom / god, so as to inform whether reaching the end alive counts as "winning".

      That's all I've got for now; hopefully, more later.
      \n
      \n
      \n

      OK,
      RE: I'm trying to think of a situation where "play could not proceed" based on a failed skill check
      But if we look at other posts on SG, you can see examples of just hat in CoC posts as well as other games. DC 30 Craft in a D&D game is an extreme example, but a DC30 Spot Check to spot the clue that allows you to go to the next scene is a more common issue. What happens when every member of the party fails? The rules might describe how to decide when to roll, they might describe what happens when you fail. But they rarely go to the point of denying the GM the ability to shut down their entire adventure by requiring success on a particular action before the adventure can continue.
      \nBut in D&D (and Call of Cthulhu where this is a much bigger problem) it is totally okay for everyone to miss the clue and for the adventure to fail. Most D&D mystery scenarios (they do exist) and virtually all Call of Cthulhu scenarios have "here's what happens if the party fails completely, or checks out of the mystery before it's solved" text. Like I say, I can envision groups for which this is a bad ending that nobody likes, and we can either proceduralize the rules to make sure those groups never get into a bad situation, or we can say some groups should adjust the rules to their tastes and the rest just proceed with it since they're fine with a TPK or the bad guy taking over the area, or the goblins eating Farmer Brown or whatever the stakes of the adventure are.\n
      Again, I wouldn't go so far to say these texts are poison, etc. But, I would go so far as to say that I have had my own fun ruined and I know of other players who have had their fun ruined by a Rules As Written legal action performed by another player or GM that could have been prevented with a slight rules tweak. My sample is not huge, but does it have to be? I don't think this is an unreasonable request. Write rules preventing the GM, or any player for that matter, from doing something that ruins the fun.
      \nBut a TPK or "you failed to solve the mystery, here's the consequences" straight does not ruin the fun of many groups.\n
      I get what you are saying JD. It is impossible for a game designer to encode the entirety of their knowledge of a game into a given rulebook. And even harder than that to regulate play outside of the shared adventure at the table. But I do not think it is unreasonable to expect that bad game play can be pointed out or even prohibited in the rules. Do you?
      \nObviously all texts, created as they are by we flawed humans, can be improved. But what you think is bad game play is fine with other groups. Have you not experienced this? I think you sort of admit you did - just above, do you think that player or GM was dissatisfied with the action they took that you didn't like? If so, why did they do something they didn't want to do?

      A game that proceduralizes-away game play you consider bad leaves no room for differences of taste. One reason D&D has been so successful over its many editions and may years is because it's very flexible and lots of groups can do lots of different things with it. (This is actually true for all financially successful games in the RPG hobby so far.) And yes, they actually contain tons of advice on how to make play good for your group. (Callan thinks we can delete that advice/instruction completely from the text without affecting play. Maybe he is right, but I don't know how we could ever test that.)

      \n Hi David,

      Yeah, an issue with traveling is that once you've gone to the effort of establishing an imaginary scene, once travel occurs again you're sort of obliged to wipe the slate clean as the character has moved away from the previous scene and you need to establish a new one.

      I'm thinking maybe make it a horse and cart/caravan thing, with 2D4 individuals traveling with the PC.

      In regard to this, my original SS examples issue is pretty much is about survival. Whilst your games issue seems to be about three competing disasters. I'd even think about cutting the monster fights entirely and have the +2 be to the travel roll itself, adding extra distance - also, so it's not undercutting the players efforts, when we divide the roll by 6 to determine the number of dice (if I'm reading you right, that's what you do) we don't include any +2 bonuses to distance gained over the course of the journey.

      Then the GM can work out the cart, it's defects, the horse/donkey, it's tempriment, the NPC's and their attitudes. I'd hinge the +2 around conversations and interactions with those people and keeping the cart moving, all of which involves a back and forth with the GM. I have a few notions of rules in regard to that, to keep it neat.

      Also I don't quite understand this part\n
      On a 6: without the potion, your mom dies; without the trumpet, your kingdom is breached; without the chalice, your god abandons you.
      \nALL of these things happen on a six on any of the dice rolled? I'm guessing that's not what you mean - but that's just a guess (and off topic to my responce to you, what accurately written procedure has over guess work).

      How's that part work out?

      \n I have three thoughts in response. It may be that only the last one is important:

      1) Oops, I wrote the rule out poorly. What I meant was that each subsequent roll gets harder. After 6 km, roll 1d6; disaster strikes on a 6. After 12 total km, roll 1d6 again; disaster strikes on a 5+. 18 km = 4+, etc.

      Those numbers are probably awful; to be honest, I didn't spend any thought on that (nor on which disaster occurs on a bad roll). My only intent was to give the player a choice (and ability to pursue a goal) that's about the fiction. In this case, it's "is it most important to you to (a) save mom, (b) save kingdom, (c) save faith, or (d) save as many of them as possible?"

      That's all I care about.

      I assume we can tweak the math until the rules actually make that functional. Start with 12 on a d12; when disaster strikes, flip a coin to see which of the destinations you're not en route to gets hit. Or something. I'm not inclined to worry about it as long as we agree that it can be made to work without much trouble.

      2) You're right, the game could be streamlined by rolling for travel speed rather than worrying about fights. However, I was hoping that the threat of death being more likely on one route (20 km) than another (14 km) would make the decision of destination order more interesting than simply going "first, favorite; second, middle; third, least favorite." Do you maximize your chance of living to have a shot at saving all three? Or do you just make sure to save your favorite, even if that means you might never get to the 2nd or 3rd?

      Hmm, maybe I need to change a rule for that:

      Each time you reach a destination (A, B, or C), your hit points return to one less than their previous refresh. 10 to start, then 9 after the first destination, 8 after the second destination.

      Again, all I'm trying to do here is take the decision about "where do I go now?" and give it the power to impact the fiction in different ways depending on what the player chooses.

      3) When you describe the +2 hinging on a player-GM back-and-forth concerning the fiction, this is where I think we get more of the type of play that tends to (a) make roleplaying fun and (b) screw up the elegance of a mechanical system. So I'd love to hear your rules notions for that.

      \n It's okay with the numbers - just plug something in and playtest, it's fine!\n
      flip a coin to see which of the destinations you're not en route to gets hit.
      \nAh, right, thought it might be that, but couldn't be sure! Entirely workable!\n
      2) You're right, the game could be streamlined by rolling for travel speed rather than worrying about fights. However, I was hoping that the threat of death being more likely on one route (20 km) than another (14 km) would make the decision of destination order more interesting than simply going "first, favorite; second, middle; third, least favorite." Do you maximize your chance of living to have a shot at saving all three? Or do you just make sure to save your favorite, even if that means you might never get to the 2nd or 3rd?
      \nOh, I didn't see that tying into the theme before! Oh - I retract what I said about removing the monster fights! I can see that tying in now. Actually - do you have to do all three things, or could you say, do just one or two and then say 'no, that's enough!'. Also just a small thing, but does the player have to decide the order in advance? Couldn't they just pick one, then choose from the remainder after? That still works if someone wants to decide them all in advance, but it includes someone who wants to feel the moment and decide from that, after completing a journey.

      I like the lowering of max HP! It sort of ties in the idea of HP attrition, without it being overly swingy in how much attrition is or isn't there. Seems a smart design move!\n
      3) When you describe the +2 hinging on a player-GM back-and-forth concerning the fiction, this is where I think we get more of the type of play that tends to (a) make roleplaying fun and (b) screw up the elegance of a mechanical system. So I'd love to hear your rules notions for that.
      \nWell, to pad out the rules on it, you can take effort into account more than just raw 'engage the GM's sympathies'. For instance, it's not too hard to roughly measure the number of sentences the player speaks (and give the requirement the sentence has to be, say, four words or more). The idea is to make a complimentry system - sure, the GM can grant the +2, but lets say the player has put in the effort of giving four sentences (which shows they are engaging with the whole thing). Maybe if they have done that many, they can roll a special 1D6 and if it's 3-5 they get a bonus of +1, if it's a 6 they get +2.

      I suggest this in because I kinda like to measure player effort in addition to ability to engage the GM.

      On top of that I'd suggest putting a time limit on these elements of five minutes or so. This is for when the game is not about chewing the GM's ear for however long it takes for him to cave in and give the bonus.

      But these ideas perhaps are quite idiosyncratic to me - you're idea with HP is cool and my ideas might get in the way of the expression of your own method.

      I'm kinda excited to see more, David!

      \n Establishing a required minimum (if you want the +2) for the amount of narration is a novel idea. We could also introduce other speech requirements, like "ask at least one relevant question".

      However, I'm not quite seeing anything to incentivize the player-GM back-and-forth you described:\n
      Then the GM can work out the cart, it's defects, the horse/donkey, it's tempriment, the NPC's and their attitudes. I'd hinge the +2 around conversations and interactions with those people and keeping the cart moving, all of which involves a back and forth with the GM. I have a few notions of rules in regard to that, to keep it neat.
      \nThe most familiar incentive to play that way, in my experience, is this:

      The GM is the judge of what would work. By interacting with the scene the GM imagines, the player can get a glimpse into the GM's mind and maximize their chances of doing what would work.

      So, the GM's rationale behind giving or denying the +2 (once the minimum sentence requirement is met, if we use that) would be "Do I think the narrated action would work particularly well?"

      Is that what you had in mind?

      - If not, what were you thinking instead?

      - If so, I think that leads to punching holes in the serial spine. I'll explain why, if it applies.

    Serial Spine Vs Lateral Dislocate

    \n
    \xab12345\xbb

    Comments

      \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nCallan_S \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nApril 2013 \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      - If so, I think that leads to punching holes in the serial spine. I'll explain why, if it applies.
      \nI'll indulge myself - I'm two steps ahead on this. Ie, how much I stressed a textually defined final say position, as well as mentioning time limits.

      But looking past that for now, I would like you to explain why (when no precautions are added) this just leads to punching holes in the serial spine. I probably already agree with you, is my guess.
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      • \n
    • \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n\nDavid_Berg \n\n\n
      \n
      \n\nApril 2013 \n edited April 2013
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n
      \n In brief, my take on the hole-punching is that once you start looking to the fiction for what players can do, the possibility space of play expands to match the possibility space of the fiction.

      The players start to:
      a) see options that are off the spine, and
      b) hunger for the ability to choose whether they go this way or that way

      A few million "Railroading!" arguments attest to the fact that players often choose to go off the spine.

      I do think it's theoretically possible that saying, "Hey, we're just dealing with the fiction long enough to determine if you're doing travel-useful stuff and thereby getting a +2 to the next roll, which you have to make," would change that equation. But it seems to me that there's an opposition there, an opposition between "treat the fiction as real" and "only interact with it from the perspective of a game goal". Can we really look to the fiction only for certain things?

      Example:
      The GM describes the nearby river, the large wooden cart, burlap bags, donkeys, and the cart driver's colorful regional attire with its tribal identifier. The player decides they hate the denoted tribe, so they want to kidnap the driver, carve the cart into a raft, use the bags and donkey skins for sails, and take off down the river to persuade a competing tribe to help them, offering the captive driver as proof of their allegiance. The player's hope then is that their new allies can deliver the potion to the distant sick mother (location A), leaving them plenty of turns to deal with the other two nearby crises (B and C).

      The GM can either (a) say "cool, I like it" and allow the rest of the game to be a cakewalk where the mechanics no longer contribute, (b) say "that's not allowed in this game" and ruin the player's engagement with the fiction, (c) try to pass (b) off as "that wouldn't work in the fiction" (which never ends happily), or (d) attempt an unbiased judgment of how the player's actions would pan out, letting it end however it ends, even if that be something that ruins the game (like a cakewalk or certain doom).

      None of that sounds serial spine-friendly to me, so I'm guessing we need to prevent the player from ever having such an "Oh, I have a unique idea for what I can do!" reaction. But if we prevent that, is there any point in roleplaying?
      \n

      \n David, so far your games questions/player choices seem to have been morally based - do you put your mother ahead of your religion, for example.

      If you wanted to argue the +2 should, in theme with that, revolve around moral choices - like whether you whip the donkey until it bleeds or not, I think you have a valid point.

      But your question seems to be, if we don't roleplay incoherantly, ie slewing across 'treating the fiction as real', 'playing to get to a game goal' and 'approaching tricky moral choices', is there any point to roleplaying at all?

      Is your example player interested in moral quandries? Is he interested in this games particular moral quandries?

      If he's not, well, that's most of your problem right there!

      If he is interested in the moral quandries THIS game presents, why's he trying to dodge the game? Maybe he didn't realise it'd dodge the game that he actually wants to play - if so, that isn't a big issue, just tell him his fiction doesn't lead to facing the moral problems of this game (the problems we presume he wants to face). Also just no, you can't - the rules don't let you (OMG, RPG blasphemy!) - the rules don't let you because they are actually PART of the moral problem to be presented. To ignore them is to ignore the touchy moral ground this game is about. If you want to ignore it, just don't play. I can easily think up fiction where it's still up to him to travel the path - but as I said before, if this player just isn't interested in moral quandries, then he isn't interested in a game about them!

      I mean, I've run into this - I remember pitching (out of game) a hypothetical about whether to steal medical supplies or let a sick village have them for free. My friends eyes suddenly light up, how he'd sell the supplies to the village! Oh, win, win! Well, not really - it was a gamist dodge of the question. He couldn't stomach thinking of a situation where you are stuck with just two lousy choices, so he makes up a new situation (one where he can sell) and ignores the situation I gave.

      Also once in a game I gave a pretty light moral choice as in someone who looked mildly suspicious was on the other side of a crowd, and about to leave - the PC would have to barrel through the crowd, knocking people down, to get to this person who might not even matter. The player proceded to try and state he used his characters drug enhanced strength to pick people up and pop them out of the way, to get through in time...

      I'm sure there are some folk reading this who love that. That's fine, but it's ignoring the situation I pitched - which was where you have two kinda sucky choices. Make a choice. You can't win and...if it seems shit that you can't win, then fair enough, the games not for you.

      So I'd pitch it the other way around - if your game is about particular moral problems - is there any point roleplaying if you're going to just go along with people ignoring it all?

      \n I think you just dodged the issues by positing a game that's about one thing.

      If I told you, "Here's a game about a moral quandary!" would you say, "I'm in!"? Of course not. You'd want to know whether it's a board game or a contact sport or theater improv. You'd want to know what you'll get to do -- run, act, or talk? You'd want to know what skills are engaged -- do you think creatively, strategically, or socially?

      My example game here is about moral choice as acted upon strategically, explored through the medium of roleplay. That whole thing is the appealing package; no part of that is a mere means to an end.

      So, if you're a player who wants all of those things, and you get to the moment in my last example where you have a neat idea about making your donkey-hide boat, what are your options?

      If you veer off on the boat, you're exploring through roleplay, but you may lose the moral choice and game-mechanical strategy.

      If you decline to go boating, then you keep the moral choice and strategy, but are you still exploring through roleplay? Or are you just tacking roleplay onto a board game, such that, y'know, why bother? I'd really like to hear what you think about this. My instinctive "not fun roleplay" alarms are going off, but perhaps that instinct is mistaken...

      As for bad players: if the player is torn between two core components of the pitched game, that's not a bad player, that's bad rules design.

      The entire scope of 'what would work' logic ONLY gets to operate within the assignment of +2.

      \nWell said. I got that eventually, and I think it's a neat idea, and all I really meant to ask* is, "So would that be fun for people who like roleplaying?" I just wanted to hear what you thought on that score.

      My first reaction is that it doesn't sound fun, but I'm trying to keep an open mind, figure out why my first reaction is what it is, and maybe move past it. I thought maybe hearing your thoughts on how "the fun of roleplay" connects to "use 'what would work' logic only for a +2" would help. But I still haven't heard them.

      I guess I should just try to whittle down my own criteria for "the fun of roleplay".

      Making roleplay choices that influence neither the mechanics nor later fiction? Definitely no "roleplay fun" for me.

      Making roleplay choices that greatly influence important mechanics and where the fiction goes? Definite "roleplay fun" for me.

      Making roleplay choices that greatly influence where the fiction goes, but don't impact the mechanics? Yeah, I've gotten "roleplay fun" out of that.

      Making roleplay choices that greatly influence important mechanics, but don't impact where the fiction goes? Hmm. Do dungeon crawls heavy on fictional positioning count? I've played a lot of those. I've gotten "roleplay fun" from a few sources in those cases:

      1) Like-minded players who drape fictional color on all sorts of character actions even if they don't have to, and fill in the space between fights with portrayal.

      2) GMs who really encourage and support the use of fictional facts as game resources. GMs who want to reward me for being clever and creative in the "what would work" department, such that donkey boating = great play.

      So, the question of whether I get "roleplay fun" from the sort of dungeon crawl I played in college comes down to the frequency and extent of these phenomena. How much character portrayal does the group appreciate, and how big an impact does the GM allow fictional positioning to have? If the answer is "not much", then no "roleplay fun" for me. We really, really ought to be playing a board game instead. (And, in fact, my D&D groups that leaned in that direction did have more fun playing board games.)

      Conclusion: Based on that, my guess is that "deal with the fiction for a +2" only gives me "roleplay fun" if the +2 is a big deal and if I truly do something to earn it, something more than saying "yes" to an obvious opportunity the GM gives me. And if it comes up often. So, in our Serial Spine game, are these the case?

      Well, +2 on a d6 is a big bonus. Each roll is only moderately consequential, but over the series of them, a bunch of +2s vs zero +2s makes a big difference. So I'll say "yes" on that front.

      There are a bunch of rolls. So I'll say, "yes, often".

      As for whether I earn the +2 by really contributing, I'll say "no", I don't think that's there yet in the rules we've written out thus far. "GM bases the +2 on what would work" is an opportunity, but no guarantee.

      Can we provide that guarantee, or at least up the odds? Perhaps we could instruct the GM, "present the player with a fictional problem you haven't already solved"? I don't think that's quite right, though. Hmm.

      Another option would be to ditch the +2 entirely and, as you suggested, bring the focus back to the moral choices. I have an idea for that, but I should probably pause first to give you a chance to respond to all this.

      \n I'm not gunna say I like definately know a solution, but perhaps something like a 'showing your working out' bonus. Like each time you describe a causal chain of X leading to Y, you get, say +5% bonus (maybe 10% - I'm working rough right now). You can even go back and describe smaller causal chains in between X and Y (X leading to X2 which leads to Y2 which leads to Y, as an example of expanding the causal description). I'll risk saying have a time limit, because wow, that sort of thing can potentially go on endlessly! Anyway, once it's done maybe roll the percentile - even if the GM doesn't pay you the +2, if you pass the roll you get +2. And maybe even if the GM pays the +2, you can still roll and if you pass you get +3 instead. And if you fail the roll, maybe you get to keep half of the percentage for the next roll (until you pass), so some amount of your prior work definately still matters in play (ie, if you talked about causal effects for five minutes, its NOT like you roll, fail and it was all for naught. It'll all add up). Perhaps put a maximum amount on the number of causal chains that can be described. But honestly, since were working with percentile, there's a max limit anyway - if you get to 100%, you've automatically passed regardless!

      I kinda think that'd work as one method - alot of gamers have a causal theory in their heads about taking actions and would like to say it. In fact I'd add to the rules that if someone argues that a causal chain the player describes does not work - even if the player ends up agreeing it wouldn't work and instead describes something else, they STILL get the bonus percentage.

      Is that anywhere near where you were talking about, David? Or am I way off base? Heh, even if I am way off base, I like the idea! Seems nifty.

      \n Very nifty! I would love to try that. Trying now to imagine how it'd play out:

      Having completed the previous phase of travel (including making a roll), the player is now ready to play for the next bonus to the next roll. "So now what's happening?" they ask. The GM describes that the cart has reached rocky terrain that's banging up the wheels. Going slowly should keep the wheels intact, while going fast risks destroying them. The NPC assistants are getting tired and frustrated and might not be giving their all right now. The GM asks, "So what do you do to improve the trip?"

      (By the way, this example sounds very much like Ryuutama!)

      The GM sets two clocks, one for 5 minutes, the other for 1 minute. (New idea. I'll explain.) If the roleplay is still going when the 5-minute clock rings, it ends. The 1-minute clock gets reset every time the player gets a +5% bonus. So if you're coming up with ideas at a decent pace, you can get the full 5 minutes, but you can't just draw out the 5 minutes by being stumped but refusing to admit it.

      The player tosses out some vague ideas and the GM says either "I don't see that working, but you could try to convince me" or "that might work, tell me how you go about it". Then the player starts narrating:

      I'm going to motivate the assistants to carry the baggage to take the weight off the cart so the cart can go faster without splintering. (GM gives "thumbs up", so player writes down a check mark, signifying +10%)

      I go up to the assistants and say, [something gets roleplayed here that I won't type out]. (+10%)

      Now that they're cheered up, I say, "Let's take this stuff out of the cart and carry it so the cart is lighter!" (+10%)

      With less weight pushing down on the wheels, I'll try to make the cart go faster, hoping that it won't be damaged. (+10%)

      Hmm, I still have some time on the clock. Okay, realizing that the assistants will lose their good cheer at some point, I lead them in a rousing song to keep up their spirits!

      GM objects, saying "You've already motivated them as much as you can, and a song isn't going to stop muscle fatigue from setting in."

      Player grumbles but there's nothing they can do, so they try to think of something else.

      The GM judges that the player's idea, though it wouldn't work, was a solid effort, and thus gives them a check mark on the GM's sheet, signifying +5%. If the player had said something utterly nonsensical in a transparent attempt to get a bonus, the GM would not have made a check mark. (We need some standards, right? We don't want players to just spew bullshit fiction that no one enjoys and give themselves bonuses for 5 minutes.)

      After a minute of no luck, the player can't think of a better idea than singing, the 1-minute clock rings and the scene is over. (Or, if the player is a good sport, they admit after 20 seconds that they're stumped, and the scene ends then.) The player has 4 check marks (40%) and the GM has one (5%), so the player rolls percentile dice hoping for 45 or less.

      Would that be fun? Hmm. I'll have to think about that. I know some players whose fun would be ruined by any sort of ticking clock. Not sure if there's a way around that.

      \n Just to put an alternate idea on the table, from the angle of emphasizing the moral choices:

      In character creation, fill in each of the following blanks with an adjective, describing why you won't do that, based on your values.

      Abandoning your sick mother would be (1) __________.
      Letting your kingdom be breached by invaders would be (2) __________.
      Forsaking your god would be (3) __________.

      When it's time to make your journey progress rolls, for each roll you get a +2 if your character takes action to speed the journey, as long as that action is (1), (2), or (3). (Maybe the GM should provide obvious, escalating opportunities for these; maybe not.)

      The GM sets two clocks, one for 5 minutes, the other for 1 minute. (New idea. I'll explain.) If the roleplay is still going when the 5-minute clock rings, it ends. So if you're coming up with ideas at a decent pace, you can get the full 5 minutes, but you can't just draw out the 5 minutes by being stumped but refusing to admit it.
      \nThat's an interesting one! I'd make it an extra two minutes, because it's hard to say anything in one minute - but playtesting could prove me wrong on that!\n
      I'm going to motivate the assistants to carry the baggage to take the weight off the cart so the cart can go faster without splintering. (GM gives "thumbs up", so player writes down a check mark, signifying +10%)
      \nEh, GM's thumbs up seems to be relevant here? I chose cause and effect because structurally it's not too hard to count those. Ie, you don't need a GM's thumbs up at all.

      You could partially include a GM by, say, the base bonus is +5%, but if he gives a thumbs up it's +10%?

      Basically there's like a spectrum between two extremes of the GM is utterly in charge Vs the other end of the spectrum where mechanics are utterly in charge. I pitch ideas like this so as to suggest something in between the two extremes - it's neither all about the GM, nor is it all about the mechanics.

      Depends what you're gunning for.

      Or is that what your example was showing already? If so, oops on my part and okay, cool then!\n
      The player has 4 check marks (40%) and the GM has one (5%), so the player rolls percentile dice hoping for 45 or less.

      \n I inserted the GM thumbs up because I've seen players ramble out some fiction that no one really enjoyed if it got them a bonus. But maybe you're right about too much "GM is in charge" being an issue. The GM who gives everything a thumbs-down and declares much of it to be "not even a serious effort" -- this guy could be a much worse problem than the player who spouts bad fiction. I'm guessing he's less frequent, though. Hmm. Not sure what the ideal balance is.

      I also worry there's not enough for the GM to do, but we can revisit that later.\n
      I'd make it an extra two minutes, because it's hard to say anything in one minute - but playtesting could prove me wrong on that!
      \nI've done some looking at the clock on this stuff, and over a minute is pretty common when a player is asking questions and trying to form a basic direction. Once they've got their first idea, though, then the ideas tend to follow each other rapidly, with pauses rarely exceeding 5 seconds. I was trying to pick a time that would work for both "get started" and "peter out", just to keep things simple, but maybe that's not worth it. Maybe instead:

      - 5 minute timer for the whole process
      - once the player finishes asking and pondering and declares "here's what my character does", a second timer is brought out
      - whenever the player earns a bonus, set the second timer (or reset it) for 1 minute
      - when either timer rings, the scene is over

      Alternatively, a much shorter second timer (say, 20 seconds) could be set to measure pauses, and stopped when someone resumes narrating, so a narrator on a roll can have as much time as they need; but I think that'd just lead to a lot of grasping and abortive brainstorming out loud, to prevent the clock from starting.

      Hopefully 1 minute is enough for a brief pause and then the full narration of the next idea.\n
      Kinda me, but I'd consider a wooden spoon award of +1% or something for admitting defeat and by doing so helping the pace of the game.
      \nOh, good call! That award could be way bigger and I'd still be cool with it. Perhaps +7%? So it's still less than if you could churn out another good idea, or two okay-but-not-quite ideas, but it's better than struggling just to come up with one more okay-but-not-quite idea?

      P.S. I'm going to be away for the next 5 days, but will come back to this when I return.

      \n Matrixgamer a short while back gave a mechanic to help get fictional material into usage - anyone could challenge the material and the person who said it would have to roll some dice, pass a target number, but it gave them a pretty good chance of getting it into the fiction. Even if it was climbing a cliff using sense motive.

      Would you describe that as LD, as that rule doesn't describe the limits of tolerance of the social contract, WM? Someone can protest, but if the die roll passes, it's in - there are no limits after that.

      As I've defined LD, it's in regard to procedure.

      I think you're refering to the spoken fiction level (rather than procedure) and that someone could go on and speak fiction that is dislocated from any prior fiction. LD fiction.

      \n Reading this post I'm beggining to think the creation of the social contract is a sort of jeepformed procedure. Yes, not an LD but jeepformed, in the sense that the not fully verbalized rules appear when they are needed, which could be a sort of organized disorder.

      How often do we roll the dice? The game states that only when conflict arises, puts a couple of examples and leaves the rest to the social contract. The GM enforces the rule, but so do players when it their turn and in other's players turn. The first few events that trigger dice-rolling may come as a surprise for a newcomer, but gradually the player adapts to the point she can predict when a roll will be needed and reach for her dice. But that last part is a mix of her own will, knowledge of the game, the group usual reaction, the GM's way to enforce the rules... you see, bit by bit we're going into the domain of the social contract.

      Which isn't explained in the book. Which keeps being hand-waved by designers. With exceptions that are beggining to become the norm. I'd say we should invade this domain a bit more. Of course, not to the point of pointing exact table manners (though I've readed a few japanese games that elegantly and politely do so and make absolute sense) nor brainwashing GMs and players to play your game without adding their creativity - dunno about you but this surely would take out my kind of fun out of RPGs - but into another idea.

      Which I think would be to provide a zero procedure in the rules to help create a healthy social contract, able to bring the players and GM into the kind of atmosphere the designer had in mind when she designed the game... only for the first ten minutes of the first session. After that, the zero procedure is to be discarded, in the same way you remove the safety net in the circus and let the breathtaking spectacle begin. Play unsafe, keep the rules, use whatever you liked or your own experience with other games, etc.

      (And please stop thinking for other people, it really gets on their nerves. We realize you are brilliant and how our slow thinking gets on your nerves, but seriously, whenever you go and outhink people, you put words in their mouths that -yes- they may have thought, but didn't say for a good reason. I mean no offense, I'm not offended and seems nobody here is, but that doesn't mean you don't hit a few nerves when you do this)

      \n Awhile back on RPG net I suggested an idea of writing a session much like a pick a path book. But if people had some idea for an option that wasn't available, there would be some arrangement in the text (perhaps if all players want to or something) for play to go 'off road'. Though I couldn't figure any means of getting it back on the road.

      In other words I think it's very worthwhile considering blends like the ten minute idea.
